(988) وركنه الكف عن قضاء شهوتى البطن والفرج وما الْحِقَ بهما. ترجمه : (۹۸۸) اور روزہ کارکن ہے پیٹ اور شرمگاہ کی شہوت پوری کرنے سے رکنا، اور جوان دونوں کے ساتھ لاحق کیا ہو اس سے بھی رکنا ۔ تشریح نیت کے علاوہ روزے کے تین فرائض ہیں۔ [1] ایک تو یہ ہے کہ پیٹ کی شہوت پوری کرنے سے رکے، مثلا کھانے پینے اور دوا سے رکے۔[۲] دوسرا فرض یہ ہے کہ شرمگاہ کی شہوت پوری کرنے سے رکے ، مثلا جماع نہ کرے۔[۳] تیسرا فرض یہ ہے کہ جو پیٹ یا شرمگاہ کے ساتھ ملحق مانا ہواس سے بھی رکے ہمثلا پیٹ کے ساتھ دماغ کو لاحق کیا گیا ہے، چنا نچہ دماغ میں بھی دوا پہنچے گی تو روزہ ٹوٹ جائے گا ، اس لئے روزے کی حالت میں دماغ میں دوائی پہونچانے سے رکے۔ اسی طرح شرمگاہ کے ساتھ دبر کو لاحق کیا گیا ہے، چنانچہ دبر میں دخول کرے گا اور انزال ہوگا تو روزہ ٹوٹ جائے گا اس لئے ان ملحقات سے بھی رکے۔ وجه : (۱) کھانے سے رکے اس کے لئے یہ آیت ہے۔ و كـلـو اشربوا حتى يتبين لكم الخيط الابيض من الخيط الاسود من الفجر ثم اتموا الصيام الى الليل - ( آیت ۱۸۷ ، سورۃ البقرۃ ۲) اس آیت میں ہے کہ دن بھر کھانے سے رکے ۔ (۲) جماع سے رکے اس کے لئے یہ آیت ہے۔ احل لكم ليلة الصيام الرفث الى نسائكم ( آیت ۱۸۷ ، سورۃ البقرۃ ۲) اس آیت میں ہے کہ رات میں جماع جائز ہے اور دن میں نہیں۔ (۳) اور دماغ پیٹ کے ساتھ لاحق کیا گیا ہے اس کے لئے یہ قول صحابی ہے۔ قال ابن عباس و عكرمة الصوم مما دخل وليس مما خرج. (بخاری شریف ، باب الحجامة والقین للصائم ص ۲۶۰ نمبر ۱۹۳۸رسن سیتی ، باب الافطار بالطعام و بغیر الطعام اذا از درده عامدا او بالقعوط والاحتقان وغیر ذلک مما يدخل جوفه باختیاره، ج رابع ، ص ۲۳۵، نمبر ۸۲۵۳) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ کوئی چیز داخل ہو جائے تو اس سے روزہ ٹوٹ جائے گا اور داخل ہونے کا مطلب پیٹ میں یا دماغ میں داخل ہونا ہے جو اصل ہیں۔
(988) Its pillar is refraining from satisfying the desires of the stomach and the private parts, and what is annexed to them. Commentary: Besides intention, the fast has three obligatory acts (fara'id). [1] Refraining from satisfying the desire of the stomach, e.g., abstaining from food, drink, and medicine. [2] Refraining from satisfying the desire of the private parts, e.g., not having sexual intercourse. [3] Refraining from what is considered annexed to the stomach or private parts: for example, the brain is annexed to the stomach; so if medicine reaches the brain, the fast is broken; similarly, the rectum is annexed to the private parts; so if insertion occurs in the rectum and ejaculation happens, the fast is broken. Proofs: (1) For refraining from eating: the verse 'Eat and drink until the white thread becomes distinct to you from the black thread of dawn; then complete the fast until the night.' (Surah al-Baqarah 2:187) (2) For refraining from intercourse: the verse 'It is permitted for you on the night of the fast to have relations with your wives.' (2:187) This indicates that it is permissible at night but not during the day. (3) For the brain being annexed to the stomach: the saying of Ibn Abbas and Ikrimah: 'Fasting is from what enters, not from what exits.' (Bukhari, Sunan al-Kubra) This effect shows that if something enters, it breaks the fast, and 'entering' means entering the stomach or brain, which are the primary areas.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
Passage type: ruling with proof. This passage defines the rukn (pillar) of fasting according to the Hanafi school: abstaining from fulfilling the desires of the stomach (eating, drinking, medicine) and the private parts (sexual intercourse), and also from anything annexed to these two. The commentary explains that the brain is annexed to the stomach (so medicine entering the brain breaks the fast) and the rectum is annexed to the private parts (so anal insertion with ejaculation breaks the fast). Proofs include Quran 2:187 for eating and intercourse, and the athar (saying) of Ibn Abbas and Ikrimah that 'fasting is from what enters, not what exits,' which establishes the principle that anything that enters the body through any orifice (mouth, nose, ears, anus, etc.) that reaches the stomach or brain invalidates the fast. This is a key Hanafi principle in understanding nullifiers.

Key Terms
ركن (rukn): The essential element without which the act is invalid; here, the essence of fasting is refraining from these things.
ملحق (mulhaq): Something annexed or attached to an original, sharing its ruling. The brain is annexed to the stomach, and the rectum to the private parts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Yes, because the rectum is annexed to the private parts.
C) No, because medicine is not food.
D) Yes, only if it reaches the stomach.

Key Takeaway
The pillar of fasting is refraining from desires of stomach and private parts, and anything annexed to them, with the principle that 'fasting is from what enters'.

The Legal Effect of Fasting: Discharge and Reward
Ruling statement on the hukm of fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two components of the hukm of fasting as described in the Hanafi tradition and apply them to determine when an obligation is discharged.

Core Idea
Fasting removes liability and brings reward – both are the hukm.

Fasting Ramadan is a fundamental obligation. But what exactly happens when you complete it? This short passage from our text gives the essential answer: the obligation falls from your liability (dhimma) and you are promised reward. That is the legal effect. In this lesson, you will unpack these two dimensions and understand what they mean for your practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its classification
The act is the fast of Ramadan, classified as wajib (obligatory) in the Hanafi school. The passage refers to it as 'al-wajib'.
In Hanafi terminology, fard and wajib are distinct; here 'wajib' is used in a general sense for obligation.
2
Distinguish the two aspects of the hukm
The passage gives two consequences: dunyawi – removal of liability from the dhimma; and ukhrawi – reward in the afterlife. Both are part of the hukm.
3
Understand the concept of dhimma
Dhimma is the legal capacity that bears obligations. When the fast is performed correctly, the obligation is 'fallen' from the dhimma, meaning it is fully discharged.
This is a key Hanafi legal concept.
4
Apply to a hypothetical
If a person fasts with correct intention and conditions, then in this world they are no longer responsible for that day's fast, and in the Hereafter they will be rewarded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(989) وحكمه سقوط الواجب عن الذمة والثواب في الآخرة .والله اعلم. ترجمه : (۹۸۹) اور روزہ کا حکم ہے ذمہ سے واجب کا ساقط ہونا ، اور آخرت کا ثواب، واللہ اعلم۔ تشریح : ادا کرنے کا حکم یہ ہے کہ آدمی کے ذمے سے روزہ ساقط ہو جائے گا، اور آخرت میں ثواب ملے گا۔
(989) Its ruling is the dropping of what is obligatory from the liability, and reward in the afterlife. And Allah knows best. Commentary: The ruling of performing it is that the fast will be dropped from the person's liability, and he will receive reward in the Hereafter.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
Passage type: ruling statement. This short paragraph concludes the discussion on fasting by stating the hukm (legal effect) of properly performed fasting: it removes the obligation from one's liability (dhimma) in this world, and the person is promised reward (thawab) in the Hereafter. The author adds 'Wallahu a'lam' (Allah knows best) as a sign of humility. This is a standard definition of hukm in Hanafi fiqh: the consequence of fulfilling a wajib is that the obligation is discharged and reward is earned.

Key Terms
حكم (hukm): The legal consequence or effect of an act. Here, the effect of performing the obligatory fast is the removal of the liability and reward.
ذمة (dhimma): Liability or responsibility; the obligation of the fast is said to be on one's dhimma until performed.

Key Takeaway

Pillar and Ruling of Fasting
Defining the rukn and hukm of fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the pillar of fasting, explain its three components, and identify the Quranic and Companion evidence.

Core Idea
The pillar of fasting is abstention from the two desires and their annexes, proven by the Quran and the saying of Ibn Abbas.

In this lesson, we examine the definition of the fast's pillar and its ruling according to the Hanafi school. The text clarifies that being junub does not invalidate the fast, and that abstention from eating, drinking, intercourse, and anything that enters the body through natural openings constitutes the essence of fasting. The evidence is drawn from the Quran and the practice of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Fasting in Ramadan is fard (obligatory). The text here discusses the pillar (rukn) and the consequence of fulfilling it.
The ruling (hukm) of a valid fast is the dropping of the obligation and reward.
2
Determine the pillar (rukn)
The rukn is abstention (al-imsak) from satisfying the two desires: that of the stomach (food, drink, medicine) and that of the private parts (intercourse), and from anything annexed to them (e.g., brain, anus).
The three aspects listed in the commentary are all part of this single rukn.
3
Examine the evidence
For abstaining from eating and drinking, the verse: 'Then complete the fast until night' (2:187). For intercourse, the same verse implies day is forbidden. For annexes, the saying of Ibn Abbas: 'Fasting pertains to what enters, not to what exits.'
The evidence is from the Quran and qawl sahabi (saying of a Companion).
4
Understand the ruling (hukm)
The ruling of a valid fast is that the obligation is lifted from the person's responsibility and they are entitled to reward in the Hereafter.
This is the consequence of fulfilling the rukn with its conditions.
5
Note the negative condition
Being free from janabah (major ritual impurity) is NOT a condition for validity. One may start the fast while junub, but must perform ghusl before prayer.
This is explicitly stated at the beginning of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يشترط الخلو عن الجنابة. (۹۸۸) وركنه الكف عن قضاء شهوتى البطن والفرج وما الحق بهما. (۹۸۹) وحكمه سقوط الواجب عن الذمة والثواب في الآخرة .والله اعلم. لغت : جنابت اور جماع میں فرق ہے، مرد کی شرمگاہ عورت کی شرمگاہ میں داخل ہو اس کو جماع کہتے ہیں، اگر یہ دن میں پایا گیا تو روزہ ٹوٹ جائے گا۔ اور رات میں جماع کیا تھا اور ابھی غسل نہیں کیا تھا اور جنبی اور نا پاک حالت میں فجر کے وقت تھا تو اس سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا، یا دن میں سویا ہوا تھا کہ احتلام ہو گیا ، اور آدمی جنبی ہو گیا اس جنبی ہونے سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔ ترجمه : (۹۸۸) اور روزہ کارکن ہے پیٹ اور شرمگاہ کی شہوت پوری کرنے سے رکنا، اور جوان دونوں کے ساتھ لاحق کیا ہو اس سے بھی رکنا ۔ تشریح نیت کے علاوہ روزے کے تین فرائض ہیں۔ [1] ایک تو یہ ہے کہ پیٹ کی شہوت پوری کرنے سے رکے، مثلا کھانے پینے اور دوا سے رکے۔[۲] دوسرا فرض یہ ہے کہ شرمگاہ کی شہوت پوری کرنے سے رکے ، مثلا جماع نہ کرے۔[۳] تیسرا فرض یہ ہے کہ جو پیٹ یا شرمگاہ کے ساتھ ملحق مانا ہواس سے بھی رکے ہمثلا پیٹ کے ساتھ دماغ کو لاحق کیا گیا ہے، چنا نچہ دماغ میں بھی دوا پہنچے گی تو روزہ ٹوٹ جائے گا ، اس لئے روزے کی حالت میں دماغ میں دوائی پہونچانے سے رکے۔ اسی طرح شرمگاہ کے ساتھ دبر کو لاحق کیا گیا ہے، چنانچہ دبر میں دخول کرے گا اور انزال ہوگا تو روزہ ٹوٹ جائے گا اس لئے ان ملحقات سے بھی رکے۔ وجه : (۱) کھانے سے رکے اس کے لئے یہ آیت ہے۔ و كلوا اشربوا حتى يتبين لكم الخيط الابيض من الخيط الاسود من الفجر ثم اتموا الصيام الى الليل - ( آیت ۱۸۷ ، سورۃ البقرۃ ۲) اس آیت میں ہے کہ دن بھر کھانے سے رکے ۔ (۲) جماع سے رکے اس کے لئے یہ آیت ہے۔ احل لكم ليلة الصيام الرفث الى نسائكم ( آیت ۱۸۷ ، سورۃ البقرۃ ۲) اس آیت میں ہے کہ رات میں جماع جائز ہے اور دن میں نہیں۔ (۳) اور دماغ پیٹ کے ساتھ لاحق کیا گیا ہے اس کے لئے یہ قول صحابی ہے۔ قال ابن عباس و عكرمة الصوم مما دخل وليس مما خرج. (بخاری شریف ، باب الحجامة والقین للصائم ص ۲۶۰ نمبر ۱۹۳۸رسن سیتی ، باب الافطار بالطعام و بغیر الطعام اذا از درده عامدا او بالسعوط والاحتقان وغیر ذلک مما يدخل جوفه باختیاره، ج رابع ، ص ۲۳۵، نمبر ۸۲۵۳) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ کوئی چیز داخل ہو جائے تو اس سے روزہ ٹوٹ جائے گا اور داخل ہونے کا مطلب پیٹ میں یا دماغ میں داخل ہونا ہے جو اصل ہیں۔
And it is not a condition to be free from janabah. (988) Its pillar (rukn) is abstaining from satisfying the two desires: of the stomach and the private part, and what is annexed to them. (989) And its ruling (hukm) is the dropping of the obligation from the responsibility and the reward in the Hereafter. And Allah knows best. Lexicon: There is a difference between janabah and jima': jima' is when the male organ enters the female organ. If this occurs during the day, the fast is broken. And if jima' was at night and he had not yet performed ghusl and was in a state of janabah at dawn, then the fast is not broken; or if he slept during the day and had a wet dream and became junub, then the fast is not broken due to becoming junub. Translation: (988) And the pillar of fasting is abstaining from satisfying the desire of the stomach and the private part, and from what is annexed to them. Commentary: Apart from intention, the fast has three obligations: [1] First, he abstains from satisfying the stomach's desire, e.g., from eating, drinking, and medicine. [2] Second, he abstains from satisfying the private part's desire, e.g., does not have intercourse. [3] Third, he abstains from what is considered attached to the stomach or private part, e.g., the brain is annexed to the stomach, so if medicine reaches the brain, the fast is broken; therefore, during fasting he refrains from putting medicine into the brain. Similarly, the anus is annexed to the private part, so if penetration occurs in the anus and there is emission, the fast is broken; therefore, he refrains from these annexes. Evidence: (1) For abstaining from eating, there is this verse: "And eat and drink until the white thread becomes distinct to you from the black thread of dawn; then complete the fast until night" (Surah al-Baqarah 2:187). This verse indicates abstaining from eating during the day. (2) For abstaining from intercourse, there is this verse: "It has been made permissible for you the night of fasting to go to your wives" (Surah al-Baqarah 2:187). This verse indicates that intercourse is allowed at night but not during the day. (3) And for the brain being annexed to the stomach, there is this saying of a Companion: Ibn Abbas and Ikrimah said: "Fasting pertains to what enters, not to what exits." (Sahih al-Bukhari, chapter on cupping and vomiting for the fasting person, etc.) From this effect, it is understood that if something enters, it breaks the fast, and entering means entering the stomach or the brain, which are the origins.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents the pillar (rukn) and ruling (hukm) of fasting according to the Hanafi school. The author begins by stating that being free from major ritual impurity (janabah) is not a condition for the validity of fasting; thus, one may begin the fast while in a state of janabah, but ghusl should be performed before prayer. The pillar of fasting is defined as abstention from satisfying the two desires: that of the stomach (eating, drinking, medication) and that of the private parts (sexual intercourse). Additionally, whatever is legally annexed to these two is also included: for the stomach, the brain (so inserting medicine into the brain breaks the fast); for the private part, the anus (so anal penetration with emission breaks the fast). The ruling of a valid fast is that it lifts the obligation from the person's responsibility in this world and brings reward in the Hereafter. The commentary then clarifies the difference between janabah and jima': jima' specifically is penetration during the day that breaks the fast, but merely being junub from a nighttime encounter does not break the fast. The Urdu sharh elaborates that besides intention (niyyah), there are three obligatory acts (fara'id) of fasting: abstaining from eating/drinking, from intercourse, and from their annexes. The evidence provided is from the Qur'an: Surah al-Baqarah (2:187) for eating and intercourse, and from the saying of Ibn Abbas and Ikrimah that fasting is affected by what enters the body, not by what exits. This is an explicit proof that any substance entering the body through the natural openings (mouth, nose, ears, anus, etc.) with intent breaks the fast.

Key Terms
ركن (rukn): The essential component without which the act is invalid; here, abstention is the rukn of fasting.
شهوتى البطن والفرج (shahwatay al-batn wa al-faraj): The two desires: appetite for food/drink and sexual desire.
ملحقات (mulhaqāt): Things annexed to the stomach or private part, such as the brain for the stomach and the anus for the private part.
جنابت (janābah): Major ritual impurity requiring ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the pillar (rukn) of fasting?
A) Intention (niyyah)
B) Abstaining from satisfying the two desires of the stomach and private parts
C) Being free from janabah
D) Reciting the intention aloud

Key Takeaway
The pillar of fasting is abstention from the two desires and their annexes, and this is proven by the Quran and the practice of the Companions.

The Six Categories of Fasting and Fard Fasts with Proofs
Classifying fasting into fard, wajib, sunnah, etc. with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six categories of fasting according to the Hanafi school and identify the three types of fard fasts with their specific Qur'anic evidence.

Core Idea
Fard fasting is established by definitive Qur'anic commands; Ramadan, expiation, and vowed fasts (in Zahir al-Riwayah) are fard.

This passage introduces the Hanafi classification of fasting into six types—fard, wajib, sunnah, mandub, nafl, and makruh. It then focuses on fard fasting, explaining which fasts fall under this category and providing the Qur'anic verses that prove their obligation. A key distinction within the Hanafi school is the status of a vowed fast (nadhur), which is considered fard in the most apparent narration (azhar) but wajib according to some scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six categories
Read the first line of the Arabic text: 'Fasting is divided into six types.' List them and note their Arabic terms.
The six: fard, wajib, sunnah, mandub, nafl, makruh.
2
Distinguish fard from wajib
Recognize that in Hanafi usul, fard is proven by qat'i (definitive) evidence, while wajib is proven by zanni (probable) evidence. This distinction affects the classification of the vowed fast.
Fard is stronger; other schools do not differentiate.
3
Identify the three fard fasts
From the passage, list: Ramadan fast (ada' and qada'), expiation fasts (kafarat), and the fast of a verbal vow (nadhur qawli) according to Zahir al-Riwayah.
The passage states 'in the most apparent narration' for nadhur.
4
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on nadhur
Note that some Hanafi scholars consider the vowed fast as wajib, not fard. This difference arises from varying interpretations of the evidence.
The Urdu commentary mentions that some say wajib; the mufta bihi is often the wajib view.
5
Connect each fard fast to its Qur'anic proof
Match each fard fast to the verse provided: Ramadan (2:185), expiation for killing (4:92), expiation for zihar (58:4). Explain how the imperative form indicates obligation.
The imperative 'falyasumhu' in 2:185 is a clear command.

(۹۹۰) اما الفرض فهو صوم رمضان اداءً وقضاءً وصوم الكفارات والمنذور في الاظهر. روزہ کی ۶ قسمیں ہیں: (۱) فرض جیسے رمضان کا روزہ (۲) واجب... جیسے منت کا روزہ (۳) سنت .... جیسے دسویں محرم کا روزہ (۴) مندوب .... جیسے صوم داود علیہ السلام (۵) نفل ... جیسے شعبان کا روزہ (۶) مکروه ... عیدین کا روزہ ترجمه : (۹۹۰) [۱] فرض: رمضان کا روزہ ہے، ادا ہو یا قضا، اور کفارات کے روزے، اور ظاہری روایت کے اعتبار سے منت کا روزہ ہیں۔ تشریح : کس کس روزے کو فرض روزہ کہتے ہیں اس کی تفصیل ہے۔ [1] رمضان کا روزہ ادا ہو یا قضا فرض روزہ ہے۔[۲] کفارہ کا روزہ فرض ہے۔ [۳] اور ظاہر روایت میں یہ ہے کہ منت کا روزہ بھی فرض ہے۔ یعنی روزہ رکھنے کی زبان سے منت مانے تو یہ نذر قولی ہے اس کا پورا کرنا فرض ہے۔ بعض حضرات نے منت کے روزے کو واجب کہا ہے۔ اور اگر زبان سے نذر نہ مانے ، بلکہ نفلی روزہ رکھ کر تو ڑ دیا تو یہ نذر فعلی ہے یعنی عمل سے نذر ماننا اس کا پورا کرنا واجب ہے۔ اگلے مسئلے میں اس کا ذکر آرہا ہے۔ وجه : (۱) رمضان کا روزہ فرض ہے چاہے ادا ہو یا قضا اس کے لئے یہ آیت ہے۔ فمن شهد منكم الشهر فليصمه و من كان مريضا او على سفر فعدة من ايام اخر يريد الله بكم اليسر ولا يريد بكم العسر - آیت ۱۸۵ ، سورة البقرة ۲) (۲) کفارات کے روزے فرض ہیں اس کے لئے یہ آیت ہے۔ و تحرير رقبة مؤمنة فمن لم يجد فصيام شهرين متتابعين توبة من الله - ( آیت ۹۲ ، سورة النساءم) اس آیت میں کفارہ قتل کے ساتھ روزے کا ذکر ہے جو فرض ہیں۔ (۳) فمن لم يجد فصيام شهرين متتابعين من قبل ان يتماسا - ( آیت ۴ ، سورۃ المجادة (۵۸) اس آیت میں کفارہ ظہار کے ساتھ
Chapter: Fasting is divided into six types: fard, wajib, sunnah, mandub, nafl, and makruh. (990) As for fard, it is the fast of Ramadan, whether performed on time or as makeup, the fasts of expiation (kafarat), and, according to the most apparent narration (al-azhar), the fast of a vow (nadhur). Fasting has 6 types: (1) Fard, like Ramadan fast; (2) Wajib, like a vowed fast; (3) Sunnah, like the fast of the 10th of Muharram; (4) Mandub, like the fast of Dawud (peace be upon him); (5) Nafl, like the fast of Sha'ban; (6) Makruh, like the fast of the two Eid days. Translation: (990) [1] Fard: It is the fast of Ramadan, whether performed on time or as makeup, the fasts of expiation, and, according to the most apparent narration, the fast of a vow. Commentary: A detail of which fasts are called fard fasts: [1] The fast of Ramadan, whether on time or makeup, is a fard fast. [2] The fast of expiation is fard. [3] And in the most apparent narration, the fast of a vow is also fard. That is, if one makes a vow by tongue to fast, that is a verbal vow, fulfilling it is fard. Some scholars have said the fast of a vow is wajib. And if one does not make a vow by tongue, but breaks a nafl fast after starting it, then that is an actual vow (nadhur fi'li), fulfilling it is wajib. This will be mentioned in the next issue. Evidence: (1) The fast of Ramadan is fard, whether performed on time or as makeup; for this, there is the verse: "So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it; and whoever is sick or on a journey, then a number of other days. Allah intends ease for you, not hardship." (Surah al-Baqarah 2:185) (2) The fasts of expiation are fard; for this, there is the verse: "And the freeing of a believing slave. But whoever cannot find [the means] then a fast of two consecutive months, a repentance from Allah." (Surah al-Nisa 4:92) This verse mentions the fast of expiation for killing, which is fard. (3) "But whoever cannot find [the means] then a fast of two consecutive months before they touch each other." (Surah al-Mujadala 58:4) This verse mentions the expiation for zihar.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage introduces the classification of fasting (sawm) into six categories according to the Hanafi school. The author first lists the six types: fard (obligatory), wajib (necessary), sunnah (prophetic practice), mandub (recommended), nafl (supererogatory), and makruh (disliked). Then the focus is on fard fasting, which includes three categories: (1) Ramadan fast, whether performed on time (ada') or as makeup (qada'), (2) expiatory fasts (kafarat) such as for intentional killing, zihar, or breaking an oath, and (3) according to Zahir al-Riwayah (the most apparent transmission), the fast of a verbal vow (nadhur qawli) is also fard. The Urdu commentary adds that there is a difference of opinion: some Hanafi scholars consider the vowed fast as wajib rather than fard. Additionally, if one breaks a nafl fast after starting it, it becomes a practical vow (nadhur fi'li) and must be completed, but that is considered wajib. The proofs provided are from the Qur'an: Surah al-Baqarah (2:185) for Ramadan, Surah al-Nisa (4:92) for expiation of killing, and Surah al-Mujadala (58:4) for expiation of zihar. These verses establish the obligatory nature of these fasts. The distinction between fard and wajib in the Hanafi school is important: fard is established by definitive evidence (qat'i), while wajib is established by probable evidence (zanni); here, the author considers the vowed fast as fard based on the apparent meaning, but there is a view that it is wajib.

Key Terms
فرض (fard): The highest level of obligation; its proof is definitive (qat'i). Here, Ramadan fast and expiation fasts are fard.
منذور (manthur / nadhur): A fast that one has vowed to perform. In the most apparent narration, it is fard; some say wajib.
ظاہر روایت (zahir al-riwayah): The most apparent and authoritative transmissions from the founding imams (especially Muhammad al-Shaybani's six books).
نذر فعلی (nadhur fi'li): A practical vow created by starting a nafl act and then breaking it; this obligates completion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The fast of the 10th of Muharram
B) The fast of a verbal vow (nadhur qawli)
C) The fast of Sha'ban
D) The fast of the two Eids

Key Takeaway
Fard fasts in the Hanafi school are Ramadan, expiation, and (in the most apparent narration) vowed fasts, each proven by definitive Qur'anic commands.

The Ruling on Making Up a Broken Nafl Fast
Defines wajib fasting as make-up of broken nafl, with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on making up a broken nafl fast and explain the three proofs that establish it as wajib.

Core Idea
Starting a nafl fast turns it into a vow; breaking it requires make-up (wajib).

This passage focuses on the second category of fasting outlined in the text: wajib. Specifically, it addresses a common scenario: a person begins a voluntary (nafl) fast and then deliberately breaks it. The author establishes that making up that broken fast is obligatory (wajib), not merely recommended, by drawing on Quranic verses and a Prophetic hadith. Understanding this ruling is essential for distinguishing between fard and wajib and for recognizing how a voluntary act can become binding once commenced.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act under discussion is making up a nafl fast that was intentionally broken after commencement. The ruling is wajib (obligatory) in the Hanafi school.
The passage explicitly says 'wajib' for this make-up.
2
Locate the Three Proofs
The author provides two Quranic verses and a hadith: (1) The command to fulfill vows (Surah al-Hajj 22:29); (2) The prohibition against invalidating deeds (Surah Muhammad 47:33); (3) The hadith of Aisha and Hafsah where the Prophet ordered them to make up a broken nafl fast.
These proofs are from the Quran and the Sunnah (ahad hadith).
3
Analyze the Legal Link (Wajh al-Dalalah)
Verse 22:29 implies that starting a nafl creates a de facto vow that must be fulfilled. Verse 47:33 implies that once a deed (ibadah) is begun, it should not be invalidated; breaking it necessitates compensation. The hadith directly commands qada for breaking a nafl fast.
The hadith is the strongest explicit evidence for the obligation.
4
Understand the Hanafi Classification as Wajib
The ruling is classified as wajib, not fard, because the evidence is from ahad hadith and qiyas (speculative), not from a definitive (qat'i) text. This distinction affects the degree of obligation and sin.
Other schools may not distinguish fard from wajib; for them it is simply obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۹۹۱) واما الواجب فهو قضاء ما افسد من صوم نفل .
ترجمه : (۹۹۱) [۲] اور واجب : وہ قضا روزہ ہے جو نفل ( روزہ رکھ کر ) فاسد کر دیا ہو۔ تشریح : نقل جب تک شروع نہ کرے وہ نفل ہے، تبرع ہے۔ لیکن شروع کرنے کے بعد وہ ایک قسم کی عملا نذر کی طرح ہو جاتی ہے اور نذر کو پوری کرنا ضروری ہے۔ اس لئے نفل شروع کرنے کے بعد توڑ دے تو اس کو قضا کرنا واجب ہوگا۔ وجه : (۱) نذر پوری کرنے کی دلیل یہ آیت ہے۔ ثم ليقضوا تفثهم وليوفوا نذورهم (آیت ۲۹،سورۃ الحج) اس آیت سے معلوم ہوا کہ نذر پوری کرنا چاہئے (۲)۔ دوسری آیت میں ہے کہ عمل کو باطل نہیں کرنا چاہئے اس لئے نفل کی جب نیت باندھ لی تو وہ ایک عمل بن گیا۔ اس لئے اس کو باطل نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ اور توڑ دیا تو اس کی قضا لازم ہوگی ۔ آیت میں ہے یا ایھا الذین آمنوا اطيعو الله واطيعوا الرسول ولا تبطلوا اعمالكم (آیت ۳۳، سورۂ محمد) اس آیت سے معلوم ہوا کہ اعمال کو باطل نہیں کرنا چاہئے اور باطل کر دیا تو اس کی قضا کرے۔(۳) اس حدیث سے بھی استدلال ہے۔ عن عائشة قالت كنت انا و حفصة صائمتين فعرض لنا طعام اشتهينا ه فاكلنا منه فجاء رسول الله فبدرتنى اليه حفصة و كانت ابنة ابيها صلى الله فقالت يا رسول الله الا الله انا كنا صائمتين فعرض لنا طعام اشتهيناه فاكلنا منه قال اقضيا الله يوما آخر مكانه (ترندی شریف، باب ماجاء فی ایجاب القضاء علیہ ص ۱۸۶، نمبر ۷۳۵) اس میں ہے کہ روزہ توڑنے پر قضا لازم ہوگی۔
(991) As for wajib, it is the make-up of what one spoiled from a nafl fast. Translation: [2] And wajib: it is the make-up fast that one spoiled (by breaking) a nafl fast. Explanation: As long as one has not started a nafl, it is voluntary (tabarru'). But after starting, it becomes like a vow practically, and fulfilling a vow is necessary. Therefore, if one breaks a nafl after starting, making it up becomes wajib. Evidence: (1) The proof for fulfilling a vow is this verse: 'Then let them complete their rites and fulfill their vows.' (Surah al-Hajj 22:29) From this verse it is understood that a vow must be fulfilled. (2) Another verse says that deeds should not be invalidated. Therefore, when one intends a nafl, it becomes a deed; it should not be invalidated. If one breaks it, making it up becomes necessary. The verse: 'O you who have believed, obey Allah and obey the Messenger and do not invalidate your deeds.' (Surah Muhammad 47:33) From this verse it is understood that deeds should not be invalidated; and if invalidated, one should make it up. (3) This hadith is also used as proof: From Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) who said: 'I and Hafsah were fasting, then some food that we desired was offered to us, and we ate from it. Then the Messenger of Allah came, and Hafsah preceded me to him (and she was the daughter of her father) and said: O Messenger of Allah, we were fasting, then some food we desired was offered to us, so we ate from it. He said: Make up another day in its place.' (Tirmidhi, Chapter on Making Qada Obligatory) This indicates that breaking a fast necessitates make-up.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof dealing with the second category of fasting: wajib. It specifically addresses the situation where one begins a voluntary (nafl) fast and then intentionally breaks it. The author states that making up that broken fast is wajib (obligatory). The commentary explains that before starting, a nafl is purely voluntary (tabarru'), but upon commencement it becomes like a vow (nadhr) that must be fulfilled. Three proofs are cited: (1) The Quranic command to fulfill vows (22:29) — once the nafl is started, it becomes a practical vow. (2) The prohibition against invalidating deeds (47:33) — breaking the fast invalidates the deed, so qada is needed. (3) The hadith of Aisha and Hafsah where the Prophet (peace be upon him) ordered them to make up the broken nafl fast. This hadith is from Sunan al-Tirmidhi. Note that the ruling here is wajib, not fard, because the evidence for it is from ahad hadith and qiyas, not from the definitive Quranic text. The text also clarifies the distinction made in the previous lesson: a verbal vow (nadhr qawli) makes fasting fard, while a vow by action (nadhr fi'li, i.e., starting a nafl) makes its completion and make-up wajib.

Key Terms
واجب (wajib): An obligatory act that is proven by speculative evidence (zanni al-thubut or zanni al-dalala). Missing it incurs sin but with a lesser degree than missing a fard.
قضاء (qada): The performance of an act of worship after its prescribed time has passed, as a make-up for having missed it or invalidated it.
نذر فعلی (nadhr fi'li): A vow made by action rather than by speech; e.g., starting a voluntary act of worship becomes an implicit vow to complete it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is making up a broken nafl fast considered wajib in the Hanafi school?
A) Because the original fast was fard
B) Because commencing a nafl makes it like a vow that must be fulfilled
C) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) always made up his nafl fasts
D) Because the Quran explicitly commands it in multiple verses

Key Takeaway
Once a nafl fast is commenced, it becomes binding (wajib) to complete, and breaking it obligates make-up (qada).

The Sunnah Fast of Ashura: Status and Proof
Ruling with proof for sunnah fast of Ashura

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of fasting Ashura according to the Hanafi school and identify the hadith evidence that supports its sunnah status and the recommendation to fast the 9th.

Core Idea
Fasting Ashura is sunnah mu'akkadah; adding the 9th opposes the Jews.

This lesson focuses on the third category of fasting – sunnah – exemplified by the day of Ashura (10th Muharram). The passage presents the Hanafi ruling that this fast is a confirmed sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah) and recommends fasting the 9th (or 11th) alongside it. We will examine the primary proofs from Sahih al-Bukhari and Sahih Muslim that establish the ruling and the rationale for the additional day.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text states that the sunnah fast is that of Ashura (10th Muharram) with the ninth. In Hanafi terminology, this is categorized as sunnah mu'akkadah – a confirmed recommended practice.
Sunnah mu'akkadah is between wajib and mustahabb; omission without excuse is makruh tanzihan.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The first hadith (Bukhari) shows the Prophet fasting Ashura upon learning that Musa fasted it, and he commanded others to fast it. This command is the primary evidence.
The command is not absolute, as it occurred before Ramadan was made fard.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's action and command indicate recommendation (sunnah) because it was a practice from before the obligation of Ramadan. The command is not in the form of an absolute imperative that would make it fard.
In Hanafi usul, a command after an obligation is established may indicate different levels; here the context shows it is not obligatory.
4
Note the additional recommendation
The second hadith (Muslim) shows the Prophet's intention to fast the 9th the following year to oppose the Jews and Christians, but he died before doing so. This makes it mustahabb (preferable) to add the 9th (or 11th if missed).
The principle of مخالفة اليهود (opposing the Jews) is applied here.
5
State the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that fasting Ashura is sunnah mu'akkadah. Fasting the 9th with it is mustahabb and not required. The fast is valid even if only the 10th is observed.
This is based on the hadith evidence and the consensus of the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۹۹۲) واما المسنون فهو صوم يوم عاشوراء مع التاسع.
ترجمه : (۹۹۲) [۳] اور سنت : روزہ وہ عاشورہ کا روزہ ہے (دسویں محرم ) کا نویں کے ساتھ۔
تشریح : دسویں محرم کا روزہ سنت ہے، البتہ بہتر یہ ہے کہ اس کے ساتھ نویں محرم یا گیارویں محرم کا روزہ رکھ لے تا کہ یہود کی مخالفت ہو جائے۔
وجه : (۱) حدیث یہ ہے . عن ابن عباس قال قدم النبى صلى الله عليه وسلم المدينة فرأى اليهود تصوم يوم عاشورة فقال ما هذا؟ قالوا هذا يوم صالح هذا يوم نجى الله بنى اسرائيل من عدوهم فصامه موسى قال انا أحق بموسى منكم فصامه و امر بصیامه ( بخاری شریف، باب صوم یوم عاشوراء، ص ۱۳۲۱، نمبر ۲۰۰۴) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ عاشورہ کے دن کا خود بھی روزہ رکھا اور دوسرے کو بھی حکم فرمایا جس سے یہ روزہ سنت ہے۔ (۲) دسویں کے ساتھ نویں کا بھی روزہ رکھے اس کے لئے یہ حدیث ہے۔ سمعت عبد الله بن عباس يقول حين صام النبي الله يوم عاشورة و امرنا بصيامه قالوا يا رسول الله انه يوم تعظمه اليهود و النصارى قال فإذا كان العام المقبل ان شاء الله صمنا التاسع قال فلم يأت العام المقبل حتى توفى رسول الله (رواه مسلم) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ نبی صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے فرمایا کہ آئندہ سال نویں کا بھی روزہ رکھیں گے۔
(992) As for the sunnah, it is the fast of the day of Ashura with the ninth. Translation: [3] And sunnah: the fast is the fast of Ashura (10th of Muharram) with the ninth. Explanation: The fast of the 10th of Muharram is sunnah; however, it is better to also fast the 9th or the 11th so as to oppose the Jews. Evidence: (1) This hadith: From Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) who said: The Prophet (peace be upon him) arrived in Madinah and saw the Jews fasting on the day of Ashura. He said: 'What is this?' They said: 'This is a good day. This is the day Allah saved the Children of Israel from their enemy, so Musa fasted it.' He said: 'I have more right to Musa than you.' So he fasted it and commanded its fast. (Bukhari, Book of Fasting of Ashura) This hadith indicates that he himself fasted it and commanded others, making it sunnah. (2) Fasting the 9th along with the 10th — for this there is this hadith: 'I heard Abdullah ibn Abbas say: When the Prophet (peace be upon him) fasted on Ashura and commanded us to fast it, they said: O Messenger of Allah, it is a day that the Jews and Christians honor. He said: Next year, if Allah wills, we will fast the ninth. He said: But the next year did not come until the Messenger of Allah passed away.' (Muslim) This indicates the intention to fast the 9th as well.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof dealing with the third category of fasting: sunnah, exemplified by the day of Ashura (10th of Muharram). The text states that fasting Ashura is sunnah, and it is recommended to also fast the 9th (or 11th) to differentiate from the Jews. The primary proof is the hadith of Ibn Abbas in Sahih al-Bukhari, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) upon arriving in Madinah found the Jews fasting on Ashura. Upon learning it was the day Allah saved Musa and the Israelites, he said he had more right to Musa and fasted it, commanding others to do so. This command indicates sunnah (recommended) rather than fard, because it is a practice from before the obligation of Ramadan. The second proof is from Sahih Muslim, where the Prophet expressed the intention to fast the 9th as well, but did not live to do so. The Hanafi school holds that fasting Ashura is a confirmed sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah). It is not wajib or fard. The preference to add the 9th is to avoid resembling the People of the Book, following the principle of مخالفة اليهود (opposing the Jews). The text also notes that one may add the 11th if the 9th is missed, to still achieve distinction.

Key Terms
سنت (sunnah): A confirmed and recommended practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him). In this context, it refers to sunnah mu'akkadah (emphasized sunnah).
تاسع (tasi'): The ninth day of Muharram, recommended to fast alongside Ashura to differentiate from the Jews.
عاشوراء (Ashura): The 10th day of the Islamic month of Muharram; a day of sunnah fasting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the evidence for fasting the 9th of Muharram along with Ashura?
A) The Prophet (peace be upon him) explicitly commanded to fast the 9th
B) To follow the practice of the Jews who fast both days
C) The Prophet intended to fast the 9th but died before doing so
D) It is a condition for the fast to be valid

Key Takeaway
Fasting Ashura is a confirmed sunnah; the Prophet's intention to fast the 9th makes it recommended but not required.

Making Up a Broken Supererogatory Fast
States the definition of wajib fasting as qada of broken nafl fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that making up a broken nafl fast is wajib in the Hanafi school and identify the primary dalil from Quran 22:29.

Core Idea
Once you start a nafl fast, you must finish it — breaking it makes qada wajib.

This lesson examines a short but crucial ruling from classical Hanafi fiqh: what happens if you begin a voluntary (nafl) fast and then break it? The passage defines one category of wajib fasting — the qada of a corrupted nafl fast. We will unpack the ruling, identify its evidence, and understand why it is classified as wajib rather than fard.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is intentionally breaking a supererogatory fast after having commenced it. The ruling is that qada (making it up) is wajib (obligatory but at a lower level than fard).
2
Locate the primary dalil
The author cites Quran 22:29, which includes the command 'wa li yufu nudhurahum' (and let them fulfill their vows). This is taken as evidence that once a nafl fast is started, it becomes a commitment akin to a vow.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The operative phrase 'fulfill vows' is applied analogically: commencing a nafl fast is like making a vow to fast, so breaking it requires fulfillment through qada.
The hadith from Aisha and Hafsa (Tirmidhi) supports this analogy.
4
Classify the hukm level
The ruling is wajib, not fard, because the evidence (ahad hadith and qiyas) is zanni (probable) in transmission or indication, whereas fard requires qat'i (certain) proof.
In Hanafi usul, fard is proven by qat'i dalil; wajib by zanni dalil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واما الواجب فهو قضاء ما افسد من صوم نفل .
And as for the obligatory, it is to make up for what one has corrupted from a supererogatory fast.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage defines the wajib category of fasting. According to the Hanafi school, if one begins a supererogatory (nafl) fast, it becomes binding to complete it. Intentionally breaking it makes making it up on another day wajib. The author supports this with Quranic verses about fulfilling vows (Surah al-Hajj 22:29, Surah Muhammad 47:33) and a hadith from Aisha and Hafsa (Sunan al-Tirmidhi), establishing the ruling through naqli evidence. The obligation is set at the level of wajib, not fard, due to the nature of the evidence.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada): Making up a missed or broken act of worship after its prescribed time, specifically here, replacing a supererogatory fast that was intentionally broken.
صوم نفل (sawm nafal): A voluntary, supererogatory fast that is not obligatory by default, but once commenced becomes binding to complete.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, once a supererogatory fast is commenced, breaking it obligates qada at the level of wajib.

Mandub Fasting: Three White Days and Six of Shawwal
Lists the mandub fasts: three days monthly (white days) and six days of Shawwal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two types of mandub fasting mentioned in the passage and identify the preferred days for the three-monthly fasts.

Core Idea
Fasting three white days monthly and six days of Shawwal: recommended acts with great reward, not obligations.

This passage introduces two specific categories of voluntary fasting: the monthly three-day fast, preferably on the white days (13th-15th), and the six-day fast during Shawwal. These are classified as mandub (recommended) in the Hanafi school, meaning they bring reward but are not obligatory. Understanding these categories helps you distinguish between different levels of recommended acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage describes fasting three days each month and six days of Shawwal. Determine the hukm level: is it fard, wajib, sunnah, or mandub?
The author explicitly says 'wa amma al-mandub' (as for the mandub), so the ruling is mandub.
2
Note the two categories
The passage mentions two distinct types of mandub fasting: (1) three days from every month, preferably the white days, and (2) six days of Shawwal.
3
Recognize the preferred days for the monthly fast
The author specifies that it is recommended (yundabu) that the three monthly fasts be the 'white days' (ayyam al-bid): the 13th, 14th, and 15th of the lunar month.
The term 'white' refers to the full moon nights when the moon is bright.
4
Understand the evidence (dalil)
Although not quoted in this passage, the mandub status is based on hadith: the Prophet (peace be upon him) recommended fasting three days each month (Bukhari) and six days of Shawwal (Muslim). These ahadith provide the proof for the recommendation.
The dalil is zanni al-thubut (single narration) but sufficient for mandub.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واما المندوب فهو صوم ثلاثة من كل شهر ويندب كونها الايام البيض وهي الثالث عشر والرابع عشر والخامس عشر وصوم ستّ من شوّال
And as for the voluntary (mandub), it is fasting three days from every month, and it is recommended that they be the white days, which are the thirteenth, fourteenth, and fifteenth, and fasting six days of Shawwal.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage defines the mandub category of fasting, specifying two types: first, fasting three days each month, preferably the white days (13th, 14th, 15th lunar days); second, fasting six days of Shawwal. The author provides evidence from hadith, including the hadith from Abu Hurairah (Bukhari) about three days each month considered like fasting the whole year, and the hadith from Abu Ayyub al-Ansari (Muslim) about six days of Shawwal being like fasting the entire year. These are considered recommended (mandub) acts, rewardable but not obligatory.

Key Terms
مندوب (mandub): A recommended act that brings reward if performed but no punishment if omitted, considered of lower emphasis than sunnah.
ايام البيض (ayyam al-bid): The 'white days' are the 13th, 14th, and 15th of the lunar month, so named because the moon is bright. Fasting these days is recommended.
شوال (shawwal): The tenth month of the Islamic lunar calendar, immediately following Ramadan. Fasting six days during this month is recommended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Fasting three white days monthly and six days of Shawwal are recommended (mandub) acts that bring great reward without obligation.

The Five Mandub Fasts and Their Proofs
Listing mandub fasts with proofs and a minor ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five types of mandub fasts and state the hadith evidence for each, and explain the difference of opinion regarding the six days of Shawwal.

Core Idea
Five fasts are mandub: three white days, Monday/Thursday, six Shawwal, Dawud's alternate-day fast, and the 15th of Sha'ban.

In this lesson, we explore the category of mandub (recommended) fasts. The passage lists five specific fasts that are encouraged in the Hanafi school, along with hadith evidence for each. It also addresses a difference of opinion on whether the six days of Shawwal should be fasted consecutively or separately. Understanding these fasts will help you distinguish between different levels of voluntary worship and correctly apply the rulings in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five mandub fasts
From the Urdu commentary, list the five fasts: (1) three white days each month, (2) Monday and Thursday, (3) six days of Shawwal, (4) Dawud's alternate-day fast, (5) 15th of Sha'ban.
The first three are mentioned in the Arabic text; the other two are added in the Urdu explanation.
2
Understand the category 'mandub'
The commentary clarifies that mandub is intermediate between sunnah and nafl in importance. It is recommended but not obligatory.
Hanafi fiqh distinguishes fard, wajib, sunnah, mandub, and nafl.
3
Examine the hadith proofs
For each fast, the author cites a hadith. Note the source and key phrases that indicate recommendation.
The hadith are from Bukhari, Muslim, Abu Dawud, and Bayhaqi's Shu'ab al-Iman.
4
Analyze the Shawwal difference
The passage notes two opinions: fasting the six days consecutively (as preferred by some) or separately throughout the month. Both are permissible.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; no strong tarjih is given here.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, one may choose any of these fasts for extra reward. The six days of Shawwal can be done consecutively or spread out, whichever is easier.
These fasts are not obligatory; missing them incurs no sin.

Ashura with the ninth. (993) As for the mandub, it is fasting three days of every month, and it is recommended that they be the white days, which are the 13th, 14th, and 15th, and fasting six days of Shawwal. (994) Then it was said the best is to fast them consecutively, and it was said to fast them separately. Translation of (993): [4] And mandub: fasting three days of every month. And it is recommended that these three days be the white days, which are the 13th, 14th, and 15th of the lunar month. And fasting Monday and Thursday. And six days of Shawwal. Explanation: Here are five types of fasts that are mandub, meaning their importance in Shariah is less than sunnah fasts and more than nafl fasts. [1] Three days of the white days each month. [2] Monday and Thursday. [3] Six days of Shawwal. [4] The fast of Dawud, i.e., fast one day and break one day. [5] The fast of the 15th of Sha'ban. Evidence: (1) Hadith for white days: Abu Huraira said: My beloved friend (the Prophet) advised me with three: fasting three days of every month, two rak'ahs of Duha, and to pray Witr before sleeping. (Bukhari, Muslim) (2) Hadith for 13th, 14th, 15th: from Ibn Milhan... (Abu Dawud) (3) Hadith for Monday: Abu Qatada... (Muslim) (4) Hadith for Monday and Thursday: Hafsa said... (Abu Dawud) (5) Hadith for six days of Shawwal: Abu Ayyub al-Ansari... (Muslim, Abu Dawud) (6) Hadith for Dawud's fast: Abdullah bin Amr... (Bukhari, Muslim) (7) Hadith for 15th of Sha'ban: Abu Talib... (Shu'ab al-Iman). (994) Fast them consecutively, and it was said separately. Translation of (994): Then it was said: one opinion is that it is better to fast them consecutively, and another opinion is to fast them separately. Explanation: Fasting them consecutively is one opinion, and another opinion is to fast them together or spread throughout the month; both are permissible.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining the five types of mandub (recommended) fasts. The author first lists them: three days per month (preferably the white days: 13th, 14th, 15th), Monday and Thursday, six days of Shawwal, the fast of Prophet Dawud (alternating days), and the 15th of Sha'ban. Then he provides hadith evidence for each. The Urdu commentary clarifies that these are mandub, meaning their significance is above nafl but below sunnah. For the six days of Shawwal, there is a difference of opinion: some say it is better to fast them consecutively right after Eid, others say separately throughout the month. Both opinions are considered valid, according to the Hanafi school as presented here. The passage emphasizes that these fasts are established by authentic hadith and are recommended for extra reward.

Key Terms
مندوب (mandūb): A recommended act whose importance lies between sunnah (emphasized) and nafl (voluntary). The text says its significance is less than sunnah fasts and more than nafl fasts.
أيام البيض (ayyām al-bīḍ): The 13th, 14th, and 15th of the lunar month, called 'white' due to the full moon. The text recommends fasting these three days each month.
صوم داود (ṣawm Dāwūd): Fasting every other day (one day fast, one day break). The text states it is the best and most beloved fast to Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of fasting the 13th, 14th, and 15th of each lunar month?
A) Fard (obligatory)
B) Wajib (necessary)
C) Mandub (recommended)
D) Nafl (voluntary)

Key Takeaway
Five fasts are mandub (recommended) in Hanafi fiqh: three white days, Monday/Thursday, six days of Shawwal, Dawud's fast, and the 15th of Sha'ban, each supported by hadith.

Criterion for Mandub Fasts and Definition of Nafl
Defines general rule for mandub and definition of nafl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the criterion for a fast to be mandub and define nafl fast according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Mandub fasts have specific Shariah demand and reward; nafl fasts are all voluntary fasts without karahah.

This lesson clarifies two important categories of voluntary fasting in Hanafi fiqh: mandub (recommended) and nafl (supererogatory). Understanding the distinction helps in correctly classifying fasts and recognizing their legal value. The author provides a general rule for identifying mandub fasts based on Shariah demand and promise of reward, and then defines nafl fasts negatively by excluding those for which karahah (dislike) is established.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage discusses two types of non-obligatory fasts: mandub and nafl. Mandub has a higher status with specific evidence and reward.
2
Understand the criterion for mandub
A fast is mandub if the Shariah explicitly seeks it (talab) and there is a promise of reward in the hadith. Example: the fast of Dawud (alternating days).
The fast of Dawud is considered the best mandub fast.
3
Define nafl
Nafl fasts are all fasts other than wajib, sunnah, and mandub, but only if no karahah (dislike) is established for them. If karahah is established (e.g., Eid day), it is not nafl.
Karahah can be tanzihi or tahrimi; here it refers to any established dislike.
4
Apply the definitions
Use the criteria to classify an unknown fast: check if there is a specific Shariah demand with reward (mandub) or if it is simply voluntary without dislike (nafl). If karahah is found, it is not nafl.

Commentary
This passage provides the general criterion for identifying mandub (recommended) fasts and defines nafl (supererogatory) fasts. The author states that any fast which the Shariah seeks (i.e., recommends) and for which reward is promised in the hadith is a mandub fast. The fast of Dawud (alternating days) is given as an example. Then, nafl fasts are defined as all fasts other than those already mentioned (obligatory, sunnah, mandub), provided that no karahah (dislike) is established for them. The Urdu commentary clarifies that if a fast is makruh (disliked), such as fasting on Eid, it does not count as nafl. This distinction is important in Hanafi fiqh for understanding the hierarchy of recommended acts.

Six Fast of Shawwal: Connecting or Separating
Ruling on two permissible methods for Shawwal fasts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two valid methods of fasting the six days of Shawwal and apply the Hanafi ruling that both are permissible.

Core Idea
Both connecting and separating the six Shawwal fasts are permissible – no single method is preferred.

This passage presents two opinions within the Hanafi school regarding the method of fasting six days of Shawwal. Understanding that both consecutive and separate fasting are valid provides flexibility in practice. Let's parse the key terms and the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The passage addresses the six fasts of Shawwal, which are recommended (mustahabb) after Ramadan.
The act is fasting six days in Shawwal.
2
Parse the two terms
The Arabic phrase 'وصلها' means connecting them (consecutive), and 'تفريقها' means separating them throughout the month.
These are the two methods.
3
Recognize the ikhtilaf
The passage states two opinions: one says connecting is superior, another says separating is also permissible. Both are valid within the Hanafi school.
No single opinion is declared superior.
4
Apply the ruling
In practice, a person may choose either method. The key is to fast six days in Shawwal, whether consecutively or spread out.
The ruling is flexibility.

وصلها وقيل تفريقها . ترجمه : (۹۹۴) پھر کہا گیا کہ (یعنی ایک قول یہ ہے کہ ) ان چھ روزوں میں افضل ایک ساتھ رکھنا ہے، اور ایک قول یہ ہے کہ متفرق طور پر رکھے۔ تشریح : ان چھ روزوں کو مسلسل رکھے یہ بھی ایک قول ہے، اور دوسرا قول یہ ہے کہ ایک ساتھ رکھے یا شوال کے پورے مہینے میں تھوڑا تھوڑا کر کے رکھے دونوں کی گنجائش ہے۔

Commentary
This passage states a ruling on the six fasts of Shawwal. The Hanafi school holds that fasting these six days in Shawwal is mustahabb (recommended). Regarding the method, there are two opinions: connecting them consecutively after 'Eid al-Fitr, or separating them throughout the month. Both are permissible, and neither is considered superior to the other in this text. The author presents the ikhtilaf without tarjih, indicating flexibility.

Key Terms
وصلها (wasalaha): Connecting the six fasts of Shawwal consecutively, i.e., fasting them without break.
تفريقها (tafriqaha): Separating the six fasts throughout the month of Shawwal rather than consecutively.

Definition and Proof of Mandub Fasting (Sawm Dawud)
Definition of mandub fasting with Sawm Dawud as proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mandub fasting and identify its proof from the Sunnah, using Sawm Dawud as the primary example.

Core Idea
A fast is mandub when the Shari'ah seeks it and a hadith promises reward – Sawm Dawud is the best example.

This passage from Thamarat al-Najah clarifies the category of mandub (recommended) fasting by establishing a clear principle: any fast that the Shari'ah requests and for which a hadith promises reward falls under mandub. The prime example is Sawm Dawud (alternate-day fasting), which the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) called the best fasting. We will examine the proof texts and understand how to classify voluntary fasts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage defines mandub fasts as those sought by the Shari'ah with a promised reward. The hukm level for mandub is sunnah ghayr muakkadah (non-emphasized recommended).
Hanafi: mandub is synonymous with mustahabb.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith of Abdullah ibn Amr where the Prophet commanded Sawm Dawud. It is narrated by Bukhari and Muslim.
The hadith text is given in the source.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's imperative 'sawm Dawud' indicates recommendation, not obligation, as clarified by the context and the description 'the best fasting and most beloved to Allah'.
In Hanafi usul, a command may indicate obligation unless there is a qarina (indication) shifting it to recommendation.
4
Note the scope of the principle
The principle applies to any fast meeting the two conditions: Shari'ah's request and hadith reward. The passage also applies it to fasting the 15th of Sha'ban.
Fasting on 15th Sha'ban is cited as another mandub fast.
5
Evaluate the authority
The hadith on Sawm Dawud is muttafaq alayh (agreed upon by Bukhari and Muslim), making it reliable for establishing the ruling.
Authority weight: zahir riwayah, cross-madhhab muttafaq alayh.

The author uses textual proofs (Sunnah) to establish the ruling.

صوم داود (sawm Dawud): Fasting every other day, considered the best voluntary fasting.

Definition of Nafl (Voluntary) Fasts
Defining nafl fasts as a residual category without karahah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define nafl fasting and identify which fasts fall under this category based on the absence of karahah.

Core Idea
Nafl fasts are the residual category: any fast not fard, wajib, or mandub, and not makruh.

This lesson defines the category of nafl (voluntary) fasting in Hanafi fiqh. Understanding what qualifies as nafl is essential because it determines the reward and permissibility of voluntary acts. The text clarifies that nafl is a residual category — it covers any fast not falling under fard, wajib, or mandub, provided no specific dislike (karahah) is established from the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category
Recognize that nafl is a category of fasting distinct from fard, wajib, and mandub. It is defined by what it is not.
The text begins with 'wa amma al-nafl' — 'As for nafl' — signaling a new definition.
2
List the exclusions
Note the categories that are excluded: fard (obligatory), wajib (necessary), mandub (recommended). Any fast that falls into these is not nafl.
The phrase 'ma siwa' means 'what is other than' — indicating a residual definition.
3
Apply the negative condition
The fast must not have established dislike (karahah) from hadith. If a fast is makruh, it cannot be considered nafl.
The text explicitly excludes fasts with proven karahah, like the day of Eid.
4
Test with examples
Apply the definition: fasting on a random weekday (no specific virtue) qualifies as nafl; fasting on Eid does not because karahah is established.
The author gives the example of Eid to illustrate the karahah condition.
5
Distinguish from mandub
Understand that mandub fasts (e.g., Ashura, six days of Shawwal) are a subset of voluntary fasts with extra reward, but they are not nafl because they fall under a separate category.
In the Hanafi scheme, mandub is more specific than nafl; nafl is the broad residual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۹۹۶) واما النفل فهو ما سوى [؟] مما لم يثبت كراهيته. ترجمه : (۹۹۶) [۵] اور نفل روزے ان بیان کئے گئے روزوں کے علاوہ ہیں جن کی کراہت ثابت نہ ہو۔ تشریح : نفلی روزے کسکو کہتے ہیں، اس کی تعریف ہے کہ، اوپر جتنے روزوں کا تذکرہ ہوا انکے علاوہ جو روزے ہیں وہ نفلی روزے ہیں ، بشرطیکہ حدیث سے اس کی کراہیت ثابت نہ ہو، مثلا عید روز کا روزہ مکروہ ہے اس لئے اس دن روزہ رکھنانفلی روزہ نہیں ہے، ہاں ذلك مـمـا لـم يثبت كراهيته .
(996) As for nafl, it is what is other than that for which dislike is not established. Translation (996) [5]: And nafl fasts are those other than the mentioned fasts for which dislike is not established. Explanation: What are nafl fasts? Its definition is: aside from all the fasts mentioned above, the fasts that remain are nafl fasts, provided that their dislike is not established from hadith; for example, the fast of the day of 'Eid is makruh, so fasting that day is not a nafl fast. Yes, that for which dislike is not established.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage defines nafl (voluntary) fasting. Nafl fasts are a residual category: any fast that is not fard, wajib, or mandub, and for which no specific karahah (dislike) has been established in the Sunnah. For example, fasting any ordinary day without a specific virtue is nafl. The author emphasizes that if a fast is proven to be makruh (like 'Eid days), it is not considered nafl.

Key Terms
نفل (nafl): Voluntary supererogatory fasts beyond the obligatory and recommended categories, provided no karahah is proven.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It must be from the Sunnah.
B) It must be from the six days of Shawwal.
C) No dislike (karahah) should be established from hadith.
D) It must be fasted on Mondays or Thursdays.

Key Takeaway
Nafl fasts are all voluntary fasts that are not fard, wajib, or mandub, and for which no karahah is established.

Makruh Fasts: Tanzihi and Tahrimi
Classification of makruh fasts with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between makruh tanzihi and makruh tahrimi in fasting and apply the ruling to specific days.

Core Idea
Fasting 'Ashura alone is makruh tanzihi; fasting on Eids and Tashriq is makruh tahrimi.

This passage classifies disliked fasts into two levels: the lighter tanzihi and the heavier tahrimi. Understanding this distinction is important for knowing when a fast is merely discouraged versus sinful. We will examine the examples and their proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of makruh fasts
Read the definitions of makruh tanzihi and makruh tahrimi. Note that tanzihi is less severe and tahrimi is close to haram.
Makruh tanzihi is disliked but not sinful; makruh tahrimi is sinful but not as strong as haram.
2
Examine the example for each category
For tanzihi: fasting 'Ashura (10th Muharram) alone without the 9th. For tahrimi: fasting the two 'Eids and the days of Tashriq (11-13 Dhul Hijjah).
The hadith evidence shows the Prophet intended to fast the 9th as well, so omitting it is disliked.
3
Trace the dalil evidence for each ruling
For 'Ashura: Abu Dawud hadith about the Prophet's plan to fast the 9th. For Eids/Tashriq: Bukhari hadiths explicitly prohibiting fasting on those days.
The proofs are ahad hadiths, so the ruling is zanni in thubut and dalala.
4
Understand the Hanafi reasoning
The dislike for fasting 'Ashura alone stems from the command to differ from Jews and Christians. The prohibition for Eids/Tashriq is due to days of festivity and hospitality.
Makruh tahrimi is close to haram because the prohibition is explicit, but the proof is zanni.
5
Apply the ruling to practice
State the mufta bihi: It is recommended to fast both 9th and 10th Muharram. Fasting on Eids and Tashriq is severely disliked and should be avoided.
Some scholars allow Tashriq fasts only for those not performing Hajj and without a sacrifice.

That for which dislike is not established. (99) As for makruh, it is of two types: makruh tanzihi and makruh tahrimi. The first is like fasting 'Ashura alone without the 9th. (998) The second is fasting the two 'Eids and the days of Tashriq. Translation (997): [6] And makruh is of two types: makruh tanzihi and makruh tahrimi. The first (tanzihi) is like fasting only the 10th of Muharram alone without the 9th. Explanation: Makruh has two types: [1] makruh tanzihi, i.e., less disliked. [2] The second is makruh tahrimi, which has a greater level of dislike and is close to haram. Makruh tanzihi: For example, for opposing the Jews, one should also fast the 9th of Muharram, but if one fasts only the 10th without opposing them, then that fasting becomes makruh tanzihi. [Separator] Day of 'Ashurah and our command. Evidence: This hadith: I heard Abdullah ibn Abbas say: When the Prophet (peace be upon him) fasted 'Ashura and they said: O Messenger of Allah, it is a day that the Jews and Christians venerate, the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'If I am alive next year, we will fast the 9th.' But the next year did not come until the Prophet (peace be upon him) passed away. (Abu Dawud, chapter on the narration that 'Ashura is the 9th, p. 1354, no. 2445) This hadith states: fast the 9th as well. Translation (998): The second (tahrimi) is fasting the two 'Eids and the days of Tashriq (11, 12, 13 of Dhul Hijjah). Explanation: The second is makruh tahrimi fasting, like the fasts of 'Eid al-Fitr and 'Eid al-Adha, and the days of Tashriq – i.e., the 11th, 12th, 13th of Dhul Hijjah are makruh tahrimi, because on those days Allah's hospitality is (bestowed) and one should eat the meat of the sacrifice. Evidence: (1) Fasting on the two 'Eids is makruh; for this there is the hadith: I witnessed the 'Eid with Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) and he said: 'These are two days that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade fasting: the day of your breaking the fast and the day you eat from your sacrifices.' (Bukhari, chapter on fasting the day of Fitr, p. 320, no. 1990; Abu Dawud, chapter on fasting the two 'Eids, p. 1350, no. 2416) (2) From Abu Hurairah: He forbade two sales and two fasts: al-Fitr and al-Nahr, and al-Mulamasa and al-Munabadha. (Bukhari, chapter on fasting the day of Nahr, p. 1320, no. 1993) This hadith states that fasting on the days of 'Eid is makruh. (3) Fasting on the days of Tashriq is makruh; this hadith: From Ibn Umar that they said: No concession was given to fast on the days of Tashriq except for one who does not find the sacrifice. (Bukhari, chapter on fasting the days of Tashriq, p. 1321, no. 1996; Abu Dawud, chapter on fasting the days of Tashriq, p. 1350, no. 2418) This hadith states:
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage classifies makruh (disliked) fasts into two categories. 1) Makruh tanzihi: less disliked, e.g., fasting 'Ashura (10th Muharram) alone without the 9th. The sunnah is to fast both days to differ from Jews; doing only the 10th is tanzihi. 2) Makruh tahrimi: severely disliked, close to haram, e.g., fasting on the two 'Eids and the three days after 'Eid al-Adha (Ayyam al-Tashriq). The author provides multiple hadith proofs for the prohibition of fasting on these days. The Hanafi position is clear: these fasts are not permissible without a valid reason.

Key Terms
مكروه تنزيهي (makruh tanzihi): A disliked act that is closer to permissible; less severe, like fasting 'Ashura alone without the 9th.
مكروه تحريمي (makruh tahrimi): A severely disliked act close to haram, like fasting on Eids and Tashriq.
أيام التشريق (ayyam al-tashriq): The 11th, 12th, and 13th of Dhul Hijjah, days after 'Eid al-Adha.

Key Takeaway
Fasting on 'Ashura alone is makruh tanzihi; fasting on Eids and Tashriq is makruh tahrimi.

Makruh Tanzihi: Fasting Ashura Alone
Defines makruh tanzihi and gives Ashura as example with hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why fasting only on Ashura is makruh tanzihi in the Hanafi school, citing the hadith and the principle of opposing Jews.

Core Idea
Fasting only on Ashura alone is disliked (makruh tanzihi) because the Prophet intended to add the 9th to avoid imitating Jews.

In the Hanafi school, not all disliked acts are equal. This passage introduces the category of makruh tanzihi—a mild dislike—and gives the example of fasting only on the 10th of Muharram without the 9th. The reason is rooted in the Prophet's intention to distinguish from Jewish practice. Understanding this ruling helps you appreciate how intention and precedent shape Hanafi rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What specific act is being ruled upon? Find the ruling (hukm level) assigned to it.
The act is fasting only on the 10th of Muharram (Ashura) without the 9th.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? Identify the source of the hadith.
The hadith from Abdullah ibn Abbas in Abu Dawud.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the dalil support the ruling? What specific phrase or intent indicates dislike?
The Prophet's plan to add the 9th shows that fasting only the 10th is contrary to his preferred practice.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Does another school disagree? What is their position and proof?
The Shafi'i school considers it mustahabb (recommended) without dislike.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Which position is relied upon for fatwa and why?
The mufta bihi is that it is makruh tanzihi, based on the hadith and the principle of opposing non-Muslims.

The fast of Sha'ban or any day is nafl (supererogatory) because it is other than fard, wajib, and mandub, and they are not makruh. Evidence: I heard Aisha say: The most beloved month to the Messenger of Allah ﷺ for fasting was Sha'ban, then he would connect it to Ramadan. (Abu Dawud, chapter on fasting Sha'ban, p. 1353, no. 243) Translation: (997)[6] And makruh is of two types: makruh tanzihi and makruh tahrimi. The first (tanzihi) is like fasting only the tenth of Muharram alone without the ninth. Explanation: Makruh is of two types: [1] Makruh tanzihi, i.e. lesser disliked. [2] Makruh tahrimi, which has greater dislike and is close to haram. Makruh tanzihi: for example, to oppose the Jews, one should also fast the ninth of Muharram, but if he only fasts the tenth and does not oppose the Jews, then fasting this day is makruh tanzihi. Evidence: In this hadith: I heard Abdullah ibn Abbas saying: When the Prophet ﷺ fasted on Ashura, they said: O Messenger of Allah, this is a day that Jews and Christians venerate. The Messenger of Allah ﷺ said: When next year comes, we will fast the ninth. But the next year did not come until the Messenger of Allah ﷺ passed away. (Abu Dawud, chapter on what is narrated that Ashura is the ninth day, p. 1354, no. 2445)
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage classifies fasts and defines makruh types. It begins by stating that voluntary (nafl) fasts, like those in Sha'ban, are neither obligatory nor disliked—they are permissible. Then it introduces the concept of makruh, splitting it into tanzihi (lightly disliked) and tahrimi (severely disliked). The first example of makruh tanzihi is fasting on Ashura (10th Muharram) alone without including the 9th. The reason is that the Prophet intended to fast the 9th as well to distinguish from Jews, but he passed away before doing so. Thus, while fasting on Ashura is recommended, doing so alone without the 9th is mildly disliked because it resembles Jewish practice. The hadith from Ibn Abbas is cited as proof: the Prophet planned to add the 9th to avoid imitation. The Hanafi position is that such a fast is valid but makruh tanzihi, meaning it is better to avoid it and instead include the 9th or also the 11th.

Key Terms
مکروہ تنزیہی (makruh tanzihi): A disliked act that is close to permissible; avoiding it is better but not sinful.
عاشوراء (Ashura): The 10th day of Muharram, a recommended fast but disliked if done alone without the 9th.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on fasting only on the 10th of Muharram (Ashura) without the 9th according to the Hanafi school?
A) It is haram
B) It is makruh tanzihi
C) It is mustahabb
D) It is mubah

Key Takeaway
Fasting only on Ashura is makruh tanzihi; the sunnah is to fast the 9th and 10th or 10th and 11th.

Sawm — Fasting
beginner

Makruh Tahrimi: Fasting on the Two Eids and Days of Tashriq
Ruling with proof: makruh tahrimi fasts on Eids and Tashriq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fasting during Eids and the days of Tashriq, explain its basis from the hadith proofs, and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Fasting on Eids and Tashriq is makruh tahrimi: the Prophet forbade it, but the prohibition is not definitive enough for haram.

In this passage, we encounter the second category of makruh tahrimi fasts: fasting on the two Eids (al-Fitr and al-Adha) and the three days of Tashriq (11th, 12th, 13th of Dhul-Hijjah). The Hanafi school classifies these as severely disliked but not haram, based on explicit prophetic prohibitions and the underlying reason of hospitality and eating. Understanding this ruling helps you navigate a common fiqh issue and appreciate how the Hanafi school weighs hadith evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon and state the Hanafi hukm level. Here, the act is fasting on specific days, and the ruling is makruh tahrimi (severely disliked).
Makruh tahrimi is closer to haram; committing it incurs sin.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proofs cited in the text. The author provides three hadith: one from Umar ibn al-Khattab, one from Abu Huraira (for the two Eids), and one from Ibn Umar (for days of Tashriq). All are from Bukhari and Abu Dawud.
The hadith are all ahad (non-mutawatir), so they provide zanni thubut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Examine how each hadith proves the ruling. The Prophet's explicit forbiddance (nahy) indicates prohibition; the exception for the one who cannot find a hady confirms the default is prohibition.
The Hanafi usul principle: an explicit nahy indicates karahah tahrimi unless the evidence for haram is qati.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (cross-madhhab)
Compare with other schools. The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools consider fasting on Eids haram and the fast invalid. The Hanafi school differentiates, saying it is makruh tahrimi (valid but sinful).
The thamarah al-khilaf: for Hanafis, one who fasts must make it up (qada) if it was invalid? Actually, here the fast is valid but sinful, so no qada required. Shafi'is require qada because the fast was invalid.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Understand why the Hanafi school chose makruh tahrimi over haram. The hadith are strong but not qati al-dalalah; the underlying purpose of eating and hospitality also supports dislike rather than strict prohibition.
This is the mufta bihi position in later Hanafi works.
