الاول كصوم عاشوراء منفردا عن التاسع (۹۹۸) والثانى صوم العيدين و ايام التشريق
ترجمه : (۹۹۸) دوسری ( تحریمی ) عیدین اور ایام تشریق ( ذی الحجہ کی : ۱۳/۱۲/۱) کے روزے۔ تشریح : دوسرا مکروہ تحریمی روزہ ہے۔ جیسے عید الفطر اور عیدالاضحی کے روزے، اور ایام تشریق، یعنی ۱۱، ۱۳۱۲، ذی الحجہ کے روزے مکروہ تحریمی ہیں، کیونکہ ان دنوں میں اللہ تعالی کی جانب سے میز بانی ہوتی ہے اور ان دنوں میں قربانی کا گوشت کھانا چاہئے۔ وجه : (۱) عیدین میں روزہ مکروہ ہے اس کے لئے یہ حدیث ہے۔ شهدت العيد مع عمر بن الخطاب فقال هذان
رض
يومان نهى رسول الله عن صيامهما يوم فطر كم من صيامكم واليوم الآخر تأكلون فيه من نسككم ( بخاری شریف، باب صوم یوم الفطر ، ص ۳۲۰، نمبر ۱۹۹۰ را بود او د شریف، باب صوم العیدین ،ص۱۳۵۰، نمبر ۲۴۱۶) (۲) عن ابي هريرة قال ينهي عن صيامين و بيعتين : الفطر و النحر ، و الملامسة و المنابذة ۔ ( بخاری شریف، باب صوم یوم النحر ،ص ۱۳۲۰، نمبر ۱۹۹۳) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ ایام عید میں روزہ رکھنا مکروہ ہے۔ (۳) ایام تشریق میں روزہ مکروہ ہے اس کے لئے یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن ابن عمر قالا لم يرخص فى ايام التشريق ان يصمن الا لمن لم يجد الهدى ۔ ( بخاری شریف، باب صیام ایام التشريق، ص ۱۳۲۱ نمبر ۱۹۹۶ را بود او د شریف ، باب صیام ایام انتشریق، ص ۱۳۵۰، نمبر ۲۴۱۸) اس حدیث میں ہے
The first is like fasting Ashura alone without the ninth (998). And the second is fasting the two Eids and the days of Tashriq. Translation: (998) The second (tahrimi) is the fasts of the two Eids and the days of Tashriq (11,12,13 of Dhul-Hijjah). Explanation: The second is a makruh tahrimi fast. Examples: fasting on Eid al-Fitr and Eid al-Adha, and the days of Tashriq, i.e. 11, 12, 13 of Dhul-Hijjah are makruh tahrimi because on these days Allah's hospitality occurs and one should eat the meat of sacrifice. Evidence: (1) Regarding the two Eids, it is makruh. This hadith: I witnessed Eid with Umar ibn al-Khattab, may Allah be pleased with him. He said: These are two days that the Messenger of Allah ﷺ forbade fasting: the day you break your fast (Eid al-Fitr) and the other day you eat from your sacrifices (Eid al-Adha). (Bukhari, chapter on fasting on the day of Fitr, p. 320, no. 1990; Abu Dawud, chapter on fasting the two Eids, p. 1350, no. 2416) (2) From Abu Huraira: He forbade two fasts and two sales: the fast of Fitr and Nahr, and the sales of mulamasah and munabadhah. (Bukhari, chapter on fasting the day of Nahr, p. 1320, no. 1993) This hadith indicates that fasting on Eid days is makruh. (3) Fasting during the days of Tashriq is makruh. The hadith for this is: From Ibn 'Umar: They said: No concession was given to fast the days of Tashriq except for one who cannot find a sacrificial animal (hady). (Bukhari, chapter on fasting days of Tashriq, p. 1321, no. 1996; Abu Dawud, chapter on fasting days of Tashriq, p. 1350, no. 2418) This hadith states that one should not fast the days of Tashriq.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage covers the second category of disliked fasts: makruh tahrimi. It explicitly lists fasting on Eid al-Fitr, Eid al-Adha, and the three days of Tashriq (11th, 12th, 13th of Dhul-Hijjah) as severely disliked. The reason given is that these days are for hospitality and feasting: on Eid al-Fitr, one breaks the month's fast; on Eid al-Adha and the following days, one eats from the sacrifice. The text provides three hadith proofs: (1) The Prophet forbade fasting on the two Eids, as reported by Umar. (2) Abu Huraira includes the two Eids among forbidden fasts. (3) For days of Tashriq, the only exception is for a pilgrim performing tamattu' who cannot find a sacrificial animal—otherwise fasting is forbidden. The Hanafi ruling is that these fasts are makruh tahrimi, meaning they are prohibited but not with the full force of haram; however, they are sinful to observe. The authority weight is from later Hanafi works, relying on these hadith.

Key Terms
مکروہ تحریمی (makruh tahrimi): A severely disliked act that is closer to haram; committing it is sinful but not to the degree of haram.
ایام تشریق (ayyam al-tashriq): The 11th, 12th, and 13th of Dhul-Hijjah, days of eating and drinking after Eid al-Adha.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on fasting on the 12th of Dhul-Hijjah (a day of Tashriq) according to the Hanafi school?
A) It is haram
B) It is makruh tanzihi
C) It is makruh tahrimi
D) It is mustahabb

Key Takeaway
Fasting on the two Eids and the days of Tashriq is makruh tahrimi: prohibited and sinful, but the fast is considered valid in the Hanafi school.

Makruh Fasts: Friday, Saturday, Nayruz, Mihrajan
Ruling with proof on singling out Friday, Saturday, Nayruz, and Mihrajan for fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for fasting alone on Friday, Saturday, Nayruz, and Mihrajan and apply the exception when the fast coincides with one's habit.

Core Idea
Fasting alone on days venerated by non-Muslims is makruh unless it coincides with one's normal fasting habit.

This lesson examines four specific days that are disliked for solitary fasting in the Hanafi school: Friday, Saturday, Nayruz (Persian New Year), and Mihrajan (Persian festival). The underlying reason is to avoid imitating non-Muslims who venerate these days. However, if the fast falls on one of these days as part of a regular habit (like fasting the white days), it becomes permissible. We will explore the textual proofs and the ruling's application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the act being ruled upon: fasting alone on Friday, Saturday, Nayruz, or Mihrajan. The ruling is makruh (disliked). Understand that 'alone' means singling out that day without fasting before or after, and not as part of a habit.
The Hanafi position categorizes this as makruh tanzihi (slightly disliked).
2
Identify the exception
If fasting on these days coincides with one's regular habit (e.g., fasting the 13th, 14th, 15th of the lunar month or a consistent Monday/Thursday fast), the dislike is removed and it becomes permissible. Note that the ruling changes based on intention.
3
Trace the dalil for Friday
The Prophet ﷺ said: 'None of you should fast on Friday unless he fasts before it or after it.' (Muslim) This indicates dislike of singling out Friday. The exception for habit is derived from another hadith: 'Do not single out Friday for fasting among other days, except if it is in the fasting of one of you.'
The proofs are ahad (single-narrator) hadith, giving zanni certainty.
4
Trace the dalil for Saturday
The Prophet ﷺ said: 'Do not fast on Saturday except what has been made obligatory upon you.' (Tirmidhi) The commentator notes the reasoning: Jews venerate Saturday, so fasting it alone imitates them.
5
Identify the evidence for Nayruz and Mihrajan
For Nayruz and Mihrajan, there is no direct hadith; the ruling relies on the practice of a Tabi'i (al-Hasan al-Basri) who disliked fasting on Nayruz because it is venerated. By analogy, the same applies to Mihrajan.
These days are Persian festivals, and fasting on them is makruh due to imitation.

Source Text

Translation: (999) And fasting alone on Friday and Saturday and on Nayruz and Mihrajan is makruh, unless it coincides with one's habit. Explanation: Fasting alone on Friday is forbidden because of the fear of exaggerating its importance like Jews. Similarly, Jews greatly respect Saturday, so fasting it alone is forbidden. Nayruz and Mihrajan are festivals of the Persians, so fasting on them is forbidden. However, if one has a habit of fasting, e.g., the white days, and Nayruz or Mihrajan coincides with that, he may fast because he is not doing it for that reason but due to his habit. Evidence: (1) From Abu Huraira: The Messenger of Allah ﷺ said: 'None of you should fast on Friday unless he fasts before it or after it.' (Muslim, chapter on disliking singling out Friday for fasting if it does not coincide with habit, p. 466, no. 2683/1144; Tirmidhi, chapter on what is said about disliking fasting on Friday alone, p. 188, no. 74) This hadith states that one should not fast Friday alone. (2) If fasting is habitual, one may fast. The proof is this hadith: From Abu Huraira from the Prophet ﷺ: 'Do not single out the night of Friday for night prayer among other nights, nor single out Friday for fasting among other days, except if it is in the fasting of one of you.' (Muslim, chapter on disliking singling out Friday for fasting if it does not coincide with habit, p. 466, no. 2684/1144) (3) The prohibition of fasting Saturday alone is in this hadith: From Abdullah ibn Busr from his sister that the Messenger of Allah ﷺ said: 'Do not fast on Saturday except what has been made obligatory upon you. And if one of you finds nothing but a grape stem or a tree stick, let him chew it.' Abu Isa said: The meaning of dislike here is that a man singles out Saturday for fasting because the Jews venerate Saturday. (Tirmidhi, chapter on what is said about fasting on Saturday, p. 188, no. 744) This hadith states that one should not fast Saturday because the Jews venerate it. (3) Fasting on Nayruz is makruh; for this is the saying of a Tabi'i: From al-Hasan that he was asked about fasting on Nayruz and he disliked it and said: 'They venerate it.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, chapter on what they said about fasting Nayruz, vol. 2, p. 9739) So fasting on Nayruz is makruh.

Commentary
This passage discusses four specific days that are disliked for solitary fasting: Friday, Saturday, Nayruz (Persian New Year), and Mihrajan (Persian festival). The reasoning is to avoid imitating non-Muslims who venerate these days. The ruling is makruh (likely tanzihi, though not explicitly stated) unless the fast is part of a regular habit, like fasting the white days (13th, 14th, 15th of the lunar month) which happen to fall on one of these days. The text provides hadith: for Friday, the Prophet forbade singling it out unless one fasts before or after; for Saturday, the Prophet said not to fast except obligatory fasts, with the reason that Jews venerate Saturday; for Nayruz, a saying of al-Hasan al-Basri that he disliked it because it is venerated. The Hanafi position is that such fasts are makruh tanzihi unless there is a reason like habit. The authority weight is from later Hanafi compilations.

Key Terms
نوروز (nayruz): Persian New Year festival, a day venerated by Persians; fasting on it is disliked.
مہر جان (mihrajan): A Persian festival day, similar to Nayruz; fasting on it is disliked.

Ruling on Continuous Fasting (Wisal)
Ruling on continuous fasting (wisal) with reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on continuous fasting (wisal) and explain why it is makruh.

Core Idea
Continuous fasting (wisal) is makruh because it causes harm to oneself, even if the Prophet himself practiced it.

This passage addresses the practice of fasting continuously without breaking fast at sunset—known as wisal. While some might think this is a form of extra devotion, the Hanafi school classifies it as makruh. Let us examine the legal reasoning and evidence behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Find the act being ruled upon: continuous fasting (wisal). The text states it is makruh.
Even if only two days, the ruling remains the same.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the Prophet's prohibition of wisal out of mercy, as recorded in authentic hadith (Bukhari and Muslim).
The Prophet himself practiced it but forbade his ummah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition indicates that the act is disliked because it leads to self-harm. The Hanafi school interprets the prohibition as makruh, not haram.
The key is that the Prophet's own practice shows the prohibition is not absolute.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some scholars (e.g., Shafi'i) consider it haram if it causes definite harm, or makruh otherwise. Hanafis consistently rule makruh.
The practical difference is the degree of sin and impermissibility.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is that the prohibition is not absolute due to the Prophet's own practice, so it remains makruh tanzihi.
The mufta bihi is that it is better to break fast at sunset.

Commentary
This passage describes the ruling on wisal—continuous fasting where one does not eat or drink after sunset and continues into the next day. The text states that even if only for two days, it is makruh. The reason given is harm to oneself (the risk of death or severe weakness). The Prophet forbade wisal out of mercy, even though he himself practiced it occasionally as a special grace. The Hanafi position classifies this as makruh tanzihi or possibly tahrimi depending on the degree of harm, but the text here simply says 'makruh'. The authority is based on the principle of preserving life and health. No specific hadith is quoted here, but the ruling is derived from general evidence of the prohibition of self-harm.

Key Terms
صوم وصال (sawm al-wisal): Continuous fasting where one does not break fast at sunset and continues into the next day or days.

Fasting on Friday, Saturday, Nayruz, and Mihrajan – Hanafi Ruling and Exception
States the ruling of dislike for fasting on specific days with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling (makruh tanzih) on fasting alone on Friday, Saturday, Nayruz, or Mihrajan, and identify the exception that makes it permissible.

Core Idea
Singling out these days for fasting is disliked; permissible only if part of a regular habit.

Fasting is a pillar of Islam, but the law of fasting on specific days carries nuances to avoid imitation of non-Muslims and to adhere to prophetic guidance. This lesson explains why fasting alone on Friday, Saturday, and certain Persian festivals is considered makruh tanzih in the Hanafi school, and the exception that applies when these days coincide with one's regular fasting schedule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the days and the ruling
Note that the text lists four days: Friday, Saturday, Nayruz (Persian New Year), and Mihrajan (Persian autumn festival). The ruling is 'makruh' (disliked). Determine the level: makruh tanzih.
The opening line gives the ruling; the exception follows: 'unless it coincides with his habit'.
2
Understand the exception
The exception is key: if the fast coincides with one's habitual fasting (e.g., fasting alternate days, or on Mondays and Thursdays), then the dislike dissolves. This shows the prohibition is based on singling out, not on the day itself.
3
Examine the first two ahadith on Friday
Both hadith from Abu Hurairah prohibit singling out Friday. One says 'do not fast on Friday unless fasting before or after'; the other says 'do not single out Friday night for prayer or Friday for fasting'. These are proofs for the dislike.
4
Examine the hadith on Saturday
The hadith from Abdullah bin Busr says 'do not fast on Saturday except for obligatory fasts'. The explanation is given: because Jews venerate Saturday. Thus, avoiding imitation is the rationale.
5
Examine the athar on Nayruz
A report from Hasan al-Basri: he disliked fasting on Nayruz because it is venerated by the Persians. This shows the principle of avoiding imitation of non-Muslim festivals.

وجه : (۱) عن ابي هريرة قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يصم احدكم يوم الجمعة الا ان يصوم قبله او يصوم بعده (مسلم شریف، باب کراهية افراد یوم الجمعة بصوم لا يوافق عادته، ص ۴۶۶، نمبر ۲۶۸۳/۱۱۴۴ و ترمذی شریف، باب ما جاء في كراهية صوم يوم الجمعة وحده، ص ۱۸۸، نمبر ۷۴)

(999) And it is disliked to single out Friday, and to single out Saturday, and the day of Nayruz or Mihrajan, unless it coincides with his habit. Evidence: (1) From Abu Hurairah: the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said, 'None of you should fast on Friday unless he fasts before it or after it.' (2) From Abu Hurairah: the Prophet (peace be upon him) said, 'Do not single out Friday night for standing in prayer among nights, nor single out Friday for fasting among days, unless it happens to be in the fasting of one of you.' (3) From Abdullah bin Busr from his sister: the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said, 'Do not fast on Saturday except for what Allah has obligated upon you. (4) From Hasan: he was asked about fasting on Nayruz and he disliked it and said, 'They venerate it.'

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proofs. It begins by stating the ruling: it is makruh (disliked) to fast only on Friday, Saturday, Nayruz (Persian New Year), or Mihrajan (Persian autumn festival), unless these days coincide with one's regular fasting habit. The author then provides four proofs. The first two ahadith concern Friday: the Prophet prohibited singling out Friday for fasting unless one fasts an adjacent day (before or after), and also forbade singling out Friday night for extra night prayer. The third hadith prohibits fasting on Saturday specifically, because Jews venerate it; the dislike is to avoid imitation. The fourth is an athar (report from a Tabi'i) about Nayruz being disliked because it is a festival of the Persians. The exception for habit shows that the prohibition is only for singling out the day, not for an existing regular fast that happens to fall on that day.

Key Terms
افراد (ifrad): To single out a day for fasting without fasting before or after it.
نیروز (nayruz): Persian New Year, a festival of the Zoroastrians.
مهرجان (mihrajan): Persian autumn festival, similar to Nayruz.

Key Takeaway
Fasting alone on Friday, Saturday, Nayruz, or Mihrajan is makruh tanzih unless it coincides with one's regular fasting habit; the proofs show the prohibition is not absolute.

The Ruling on Continuous Fasting (Wisal)
Defines and rules on continuous fasting without breaking at sunset

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on continuous fasting (wisal), identify the proofs from Quran and Sunnah, and explain why it is makruh rather than haram.

Core Idea
Continuous fasting is makruh because the Prophet forbade it, but his exception shows it is not haram for the ummah.

Continuous fasting (wisal) means not breaking the fast at sunset, connecting one day's fast to the next without any iftar. This passage from Thamaratun Najah defines the practice, gives its ruling as makruh, and provides two proofs: a hadith of the Prophet prohibiting it, and a Quranic verse implying the fast ends at night. Understanding this ruling requires distinguishing the Prophet's exclusive prerogative from the general prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Read the definition of wisal: fasting continuously without breaking at sunset, even for two days. The text rules it as makruh (disliked).
Makruh here is tanzihi, not tahrimi.
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites two proofs: hadith from Anas where the Prophet says 'Do not practice continuous fasting,' and Quran 2:187 'then complete the fast until the night.'
The hadith is from Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition in the hadith indicates dislike, not absolute prohibition, because the Prophet himself practiced it and explained he is not like us (he is fed by Allah). The Quranic verse implies ending the fast at night, which discourages continuous fasting.
The Prophet's exception is key to understanding the ruling as makruh.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) permit wisal if one has strength, while others consider it haram if it causes harm. The Hanafi position is makruh tanzihi.
The difference stems from how the hadith prohibition is interpreted.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school relies on the authentic hadith and the Prophet's exception, concluding that the prohibition is for general guidance (karahah) not for strict forbiddance.
The mufta bihi is makruh; one should avoid it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۰۰۰) وكره صوم الوصال ولو يومين وهو ان لا يفطر بعد الغروب اصلا حتى يتصل صوم الغد

وجه : (۱) عن انس عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال لا تواصلوا قالوا انك تواصل قال لست كاحد منكم اني اطعم و اسقى او اني ابیت و اسقى (بخاری شریف، باب الوصال، ص ۱۳۱۵، نمبر ۱۹۶۱ و مسلم شریف، باب النهي عن الوصال، ص ۲۴۸، نمبر ۲۵۶۳/۱۱۰۲)

(۲) ثم أتموا الصيام إلى الليل (آیت ۱۸۷، سورة البقرة ٢)
(1000) And continuous fasting is disliked, even for two days. It is that one does not break the fast after sunset at all until the next day's fast connects. Evidence: (1) From Anas: the Prophet (peace be upon him) said, 'Do not practice continuous fasting.' They said, 'But you practice it.' He said, 'I am not like any of you; I am fed and given to drink.' (2) Then complete the fast until the night (Quran 2:187).
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage defines and rules on wisal (continuous fasting). The ruling: it is makruh (disliked) to fast continuously without breaking the fast at sunset, even if only for two days. The definition clarifies that wisal means not eating or drinking after Maghrib, linking the fast of one day directly to the next. Two proofs are given: a hadith from Anas where the Prophet forbids wisal, though he himself could do it due to special divine provision; and the Quranic verse 'then complete the fast until the night', which implies breaking the fast at nightfall, thus discouraging continuous fasting. The Hanafi school considers it makruh tanzihi to avoid hardship and imitation of the Prophet's exclusive practice.

Key Terms
صوم الوصال (sawm al-wisal): Fasting continuously without breaking at sunset, connecting one day's fast to the next.
وصال (wisal): Linguistically: connection; technically: continuous fasting without iftar between days.

A) It is obligatory.
B) It is permissible without dislike.
C) It is makruh (disliked).
D) It is haram (forbidden).

Key Takeaway
Continuous fasting (wisal) is makruh tanzihi in the Hanafi school, based on the Prophet's prohibition and his exception, and the Quranic limit of fasting until night.

Makruh to Fast Every Day (Sawm al-Dahr)
Ruling on perpetual fasting with one proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on perpetual fasting (sawm al-dahr) and explain the evidence from the hadith that leads to its classification as makruh tanzihi.

Core Idea
Perpetual fasting is disliked because the Prophet warned it harms the body and soul.

Fasting every single day of the year, including days like Eid, is known as sawm al-dahr. The Hanafi school considers this practice makruh (disliked), not haram, based on a clear hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) expressed disapproval. In this lesson, we will examine the ruling and the proof from the Sunnah, and understand the nuanced distinction between makruh tanzihi and outright prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage begins by stating the ruling: 'وکره صوم الدهر' — perpetual fasting is disliked. Note that the text uses 'kariha' (disliked), not 'harrama' (forbidden). This tells us the hukm level is makruh (disliked).
In Hanafi fiqh, makruh tanzihi is a light disliked act; avoiding it is rewarded but doing it is not sinful.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is a hadith from Abdullah ibn Amr ibn al-As. The Prophet (peace be upon him) asked him rhetorically if he fasts every day and prays every night. When Abdullah confirmed, the Prophet warned of physical harm (sunken eyes, exhaustion) and said, 'He has not fasted who fasts the dahr.'
The hadith is found in both Sahih al-Bukhari and Sahih Muslim, making it sound in transmission (thubut = zanni, but widely accepted).
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof indicates the ruling)
The Prophet's rhetorical question and warning are expressions of disapproval. The phrase 'la sama man sama al-dahr' (he has not fasted who fasts perpetually) is not a negation of the act's validity but a severe reprimand. It indicates that such fasting is not the ideal or recommended form of worship. Thus, the Hanafi school derives makruh tanzihi, not haram.
If the hadith were an absolute prohibition, the ruling would be haram. The Hanafi usul principle: a strong discouragement without an explicit prohibition yields makruh tahrimi or tanzihi depending on context. Here it is tanzihi because the harm is not absolute.
4
Note the cross-madhhab ikhtilaf (disagreement)
The Shafi'i school and others permit sawm al-dahr if one has the strength and does not neglect rights. They interpret the hadith as a warning only for those who cannot bear it. The Hanafi view is more cautious: it is disliked even for the strong because it resembles the practice of the Prophet Dawud (fasting alternate days) which is superior.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf): a Hanafi would discourage perpetual fasting, while a Shafi'i may allow it if no harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۰۰۱) وكره صوم الدهر

وجه : (۱) سمعت عبد الله بن عمرو بن العاص قال قال لي النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم انك لتصوم الدهر وتقوم الليل ؟ فقلت نعم قال انك اذا فعلت ذاك هجمت له العين و نفهت له النفس لا صام من صام الدهر (بخاری شریف، باب صوم داود علیہ السلام، ص ۱۳۱۸، نمبر ۱۹۷۹ و مسلم شریف، باب النهي عن صوم الدهر لمن تضرر به، ص ۴۷۲، نمبر ۲۷۲۹/۱۱۵۹)
(1001) And perpetual fasting (sawm al-dahr) is disliked. Evidence: (1) I heard Abdullah bin Amr bin al-As say: The Prophet (peace be upon him) said to me, 'Do you fast every day and stand (in prayer) every night?' I said, 'Yes.' He said, 'If you do that, your eyes will become sunken and your soul will be exhausted. He has not fasted who fasts the dahr.' (Bukhari, Bab Sawm Dawud; Muslim, Bab al-Nahi 'an Sawm al-Dahr)
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage states that perpetual fasting (sawm al-dahr, i.e., fasting every day of the year) is makruh (disliked). The proof is a hadith where the Prophet disapproved of Abdullah bin Amr's practice of fasting every day, warning of physical harm and exhaustion. The Prophet's statement 'He has not fasted who fasts the dahr' is understood as a strong discouragement. The Hanafi school classifies this as makruh tanzihi because it leads to hardship and may cause neglect of other duties. However, if one can do it without harm, some scholars consider it permissible, but the preferred position is dislike.

Key Terms
صوم الدهر (sawm al-dahr): Fasting every day of the year, including days when fasting is prohibited (like Eid).
دهر (dahr): Time, eternity; here meaning all days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is recommended.
B) It is makruh (disliked).
C) It is haram (forbidden).
D) It is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Perpetual fasting is disliked because it contradicts the prophetic advice of balance and may cause harm; the better practice is to follow the Sunnah of fasting alternate days like the Prophet Dawud.

The Hanafi Ruling on Intention for Fasting: General vs Specified
Chapter on conditions for intention in fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify each type of fast (Ramadan, nafl, qada, kaffarah, nadhr) into whether it requires a general or specific intention, and apply the Hanafi rule that the time itself specifies the obligation.

Core Idea
For Ramadan and specified vows, a general intention suffices; for qada, kaffarah, and absolute vows, you must intend the specific fast at night.

The intention (niyyah) is a condition for the validity of every fast, but its timing and specificity differ by the type of fast. This passage from Thamaratun Najah clarifies the Hanafi position on when a general intention (e.g., 'I am fasting') suffices, and when you must specify both the type and make the intention during the night. Understanding these distinctions prevents common errors, such as assuming all obligatory fasts require the same intention regimen.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the type of fast (Ramadan, nafl, qada, kaffarah, nadhr) and the rule for its intention based on the text.
The text states explicitly for each type.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof for the sufficiency of general intention in Ramadan is the Quranic verse 2:185, which obligates fasting the month itself, overriding other intentions.
The imperative 'فليصمه' is the key.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The command to fast the month indicates that the time is the defining factor; thus any intention directed at that day fulfills the obligation because the time is specified for Ramadan.
This is a strong dalala qati'a.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
For the sick person (1007), there is a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school whether his intention for another wajib counts as that wajib or as Ramadan.
The text does not give the final tarjih.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The preferred view (mufta bihi) is that the sick person's fast counts as Ramadan, because he is like a healthy person who chose to fast.
This is based on the ease of the concession (rukhsa).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيما يشترط تبيت النية
(1004) ويصح ايضا بمطلق النية وبنية النفل ولو كان مسافرا او مريضا في الاصح.
(1005) ويصح اداء رمضان بنية واجب آخر لمن كان صحيحا مقيما
(1006) بخلاف المسافر فانه يقع عمّا نواه من الواجب
(1007) واختلف الترجيح في المريض اذا نوى وقت دن
(1008) ولا يصح المنذور المعيّن زمانه بنية واجب غيره بل يقع عمّا نواه من الواجـب فيـه .
(1009) واما القسم الثاني وهو ما يشترط فيه تعيين النية وتبييتها فهو قضاء رمضان وقضاء ما أفسده من نفل وصوم الكفارات بانواعها والمنذور المطلق كقوله ان شفى الله مريضى
Chapter on what requires intention to be made at night.
(1004) And it is also valid with an absolute intention and with the intention of a supererogatory fast, even if one is a traveler or sick, according to the soundest opinion.
(1005) And performing Ramadan [fast] is valid with the intention of another obligatory fast for one who is healthy and resident.
(1006) Contrary to the traveler, for it counts as what he intended of the obligation.
(1007) And the preferred ruling differs concerning the sick person when he intends another obligatory [fast] in Ramadan.
(1008) And a vowed fast with a specified time is not valid with the intention of another obligatory fast; rather, it counts as what he intended of the obligation in it.
(1009) As for the second category—that which requires specifying the intention and making it at night—it is: making up Ramadan [fasts], making up a supererogatory fast that one has broken, fasts of expiations of all types, and the absolute vow, such as one saying: 'If Allah heals my sick person...'
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a chapter from Thamaratun Najah on the rulings of intention (niyyah) for fasting. It begins by stating that for the Ramadan fast, a general intention (e.g., 'I am fasting') or even an intention for a supererogatory (nafl) fast suffices, and this applies even if the person is a traveler or sick, according to the soundest view. The reasoning is that the obligatory fast is paramount, and the time is specifically for Ramadan. Next, for a healthy resident person, if he intends another obligatory fast during Ramadan, the Ramadan fast is still fulfilled, as the Quran (2:185) commands fasting the month. For a traveler, however, since he is allowed not to fast, his intention for another wajib fast counts as that other wajib, because that other obligation is also binding on him. For a sick person, there is a difference of opinion: some scholars say the other wajib is fulfilled, others say Ramadan is fulfilled, because the sick person could have taken the concession not to fast but chose to fast, thus becoming like a healthy person. The passage then clarifies that for a vowed fast with a specified time (e.g., a vow to fast every Friday), one cannot replace it with another wajib intention; rather, it remains the vowed fast. Finally, it lists the second category of fasts that require both specifying the intention (ta'yin al-niyyah) and making it at night (tabyit al-niyyah): (1) qada of Ramadan, (2) qada of a broken supererogatory fast, (3) expiatory fasts (kaffarat), and (4) absolute vows (nadhr mutlaq) where no specific day is set. For these, the intention must be made during the night and the type must be specified, because the day is not already designated for that fast.

Key Terms
تبيت النية (tabyit al-niyyah): Making the intention during the night, before dawn, for the next day's fast.
المنذور المعيّن (al-mandhur al-mu'ayyan): A vowed fast where the time (e.g., a specific day) is designated.
المنذور المطلق (al-mandhur al-mutlaq): A vowed fast without specifying a particular day.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a traveler intends a supererogatory fast during Ramadan, what is his fast considered?
A) It is invalid because intention is not correct.
B) It counts as a supererogatory fast.
C) It counts as the Ramadan fast.
D) It counts as whatever he intended, but the choice is not valid.

Key Takeaway
The time specification of Ramadan overrides any other intention for a fast except when the person is permitted to leave Ramadan (traveler), in which case the intended wajib takes effect; for the sick, the preferred view is that Ramadan counts.

Tabyit al-Niyyah and Ta'yin: Conditions of Intention for Specified and Unspecified Fasts
Clarifying intention requirements for two categories of fasts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which fasts require tabyit al-niyyah and ta'yin, and apply the rule that intention for another wajib on a specified vow day counts for that wajib.

Core Idea
For specified vows, intention for another wajib overrides; for unspecified fasts, night intention and specification are mandatory.

This passage from a Hanafi fiqh text clarifies the nuanced rules of intention (niyyah) in fasting. It distinguishes between two categories of fasts: those with a fixed time (like a vowed day) and those without (like make-up and expiatory fasts). Understanding this distinction is crucial for the validity of your fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage explicitly divides fasts into two categories regarding intention rules. Find the sentence that introduces 'al-qism al-awwal' and 'al-qism al-thani' in the Arabic text. The first is about al-nadhr al-mu'ayyan al-zaman (specified vow), the second covers four types.
The Arabic uses 'وأما القسم الثاني' to mark the transition.
2
Analyze the ruling for specified vow
For a specified vow (like fasting every Friday), if one intends another wajib fast (e.g., qada' of Ramadan) on that day, the fast counts for the other wajib, not the vow. The reasoning: both are equally wajib, so intention for the second overrides.
The Urdu commentary explains: 'دونوں ہی واجب ہیں اس لئے دوسرے واجب کی نیت کرے گا تو دوسرا واجب ادا ہو جائے گا.'
3
List the four types requiring tabyit and ta'yin
The passage lists: (1) qada' of Ramadan, (2) qada' of broken nafl fast, (3) all kaffarah fasts, and (4) absolute vow (nadhr mutlaq). Each requires intention from the night (tabyit) and specification (ta'yin) of what fast is being kept.
The Urdu enumerates them: 'يہ چار قسم کے روزے يہ ہيں [1] رمضان کی قضا۔ (۲) نفلی روزہ توڑ دیا ہو اس کی قضا۔ (۳) جتنے بھی کفارات ہیں انکے روزے۔ [۴] نذر مطلق'
4
Understand the consequence of omission
If one makes only a general intention (e.g., 'I intend to fast') without specifying, the fast becomes nafl (supererogatory). This is based on the principle that a general intention is sufficient for nafl, but not for wajib fasts that require ta'yin.
The passage states: 'مطلق نیت سے نفلی روزہ ہو جائے گا.'
5
Connect the hadith evidence
The hadith from Hafsa (RA) is cited: 'Whoever does not intend fasting before dawn, there is no fast for him.' This establishes the requirement of tabyit al-niyyah as a condition for these fasts. The hadith is a dalil (evidence) for the obligation of night intention.
The Arabic hadith: 'من لم يجمع الصيام قبل الفجر فلا صيام له.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيما يشترط تبيت النية واجب آخر في رمضان (١٠٠٨) ولا يصح المنذور المعين زمانه بنية واجب غيره بل يقع عما نواه من الواجب فيه. (١٠٠٩) وأما القسم الثاني وهو ما يشترط فيه تعيين النية وتبييتها فهو قضاء رمضان وقضاء ما أفسده من نفل وصوم الكفارات بأنواعها والمنذور المطلق كقوله إن شفى الله مريضي فعل صوم يوم فحصل الشفاء. ترجمه: (۱۰۰۸) اور وہ منت کا روزہ جس کا زمانہ متعین ہے دوسرے واجب کی نیت سے صحیح نہیں، بلکہ جس واجب کی نیت کی ہے وہی شمار ہوگا۔ تشریح: مثلا جمعہ کے دن روزہ رکھنے کی منت مانی اور جمعہ کے دن دوسرے واجب کی نیت کر لی تو دوسرا واجب ادا ہو جائے گا۔ وجه: جمعہ کے دن جتنی اہمیت نذر کی ہے اتنی ہی اہمیت دوسرے واجب کی ہے، کیونکہ دونوں ہی واجب ہیں اس لئے دوسرے واجب کی نیت کرے گا تو دوسرا واجب ادا ہو جائے گا۔ ترجمه: (۱۰۰۹) اور دوسری قسم جس میں نیت کا متعین کرنا اور رات سے نیت کرنا شرط ہے وہ رمضان کی قضا ہے، اور وہ نفل روزہ جو فاسد کر دیا تھا۔ اور کفاروں کے تمام قسم کے روزے، اور مطلق منت کے روزے ( جن میں کسی دن کی تعیین نہ ہو ) جیسے یہ کہا کہ اگر اللہ نے میرے بیمار کو شفادی تو میرے ذمہ ایک روزہ ہے پھر شفا ہو گئی۔ تشریح: یہاں چار قسم کے روزے ہیں جنکے لئے زوال سے پہلے پہلے نیت کرنا کافی نہیں ہے، بلکہ رات سے ہی نیت کرنی ہوگی اور اس روزے کا تعین کرنا بھی ضروری ہوگا جس کا صبح روزہ رکھنا ہے۔ مثلا رات ہی میں یہ نیت کرنا ہوگا کہ میں صبح قضا روزہ رکھ رہا ہوں، اور اگر اس نے روزے کا تعین نہیں کیا تو مطلق نیت سے نفلی روزہ ہو جائے گا۔ وہ چار قسم کے روزے یہ ہیں [1] رمضان کی قضا۔ (۲) نفلی روزہ توڑ دیا ہو اس کی قضا۔ (۳) جتنے بھی کفارات ہیں انکے روزے۔ [۴] نذر مطلق، یعنی جس نذر میں دن متعین نہ کیا ہو،مثلا یوں کہے کہ اگر اللہ تعالی نے مرض سے شفاء دی تو میں کسی بھی دن دو روزے رکھوں گا، پھر اللہ تعالی نے شفاء دے دی اور اس پر دوروزے واجب ہو گئے، تو اس کے لئے رات سے ہی نیت کرنی ہوگی، اور اس روزے کا تعین کرنا ہوگا۔ وجه: ان روزوں کے لئے وہ دن پہلے سے متعین نہیں ہے، کسی بھی دن یہ روزے ادا کر سکتے ہیں،اس لئے اس روزے کے لئے وہ دن متعین کرنا ہو گا (۲) رات سے نیت کرنے کی ضرورت ہے اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن حفصة زوج النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: من لم يجمع الصيام قبل الفجر فلا صيام له. (أبو داود شریف، باب فى النية فى الصوم ص ٣٤٠ نمبر ٢٤٥٤) (ترمذی شریف، باب ما جاء لا صیام لمن لم يحرم من المليل ص ٤٥ نمبر ٧٣٠)
Section on what requires fixing the intention (at night) and another obligation in Ramadan. (1008) The vowed fast whose time is specified is not valid with the intention of another obligation; rather it counts for what he intended of obligation in it. (1009) As for the second category, which requires specification of intention and fixing it at night, it is the make-up of Ramadan, make-up of a voluntary fast he spoiled, fasts of all types of expiations, and the absolute vow like saying 'If Allah heals my sick person I will fast a day' then healing occurred.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage defines two categories of fasts regarding intention. The first category (1008) concerns a vowed fast with a specified time (al-nadhr al-mu'ayyan al-zaman). If one makes intention for another wajib fast on that day, it is not valid for the vow but counts for the other wajib because both are equally wajib. The second category (1009) covers four types of fasts that require both tabyit al-niyyah (intention from night) and ta'yin (specification): (1) qada' of Ramadan, (2) qada' of a broken nafl, (3) all kaffarah fasts, and (4) absolute vow (al-nadhr al-mutlaq) without a fixed day. The hadith from Hafsa (RA) is cited: 'Whoever does not intend fasting before dawn, there is no fast for him.' This establishes the requirement of night intention for these fasts. The passage also explains that if one makes a general intention (e.g., just 'I intend to fast') without specifying the type, it becomes a nafl fast. The wisdom is that these fasts are not tied to a specific day by their nature, so they require deliberate specification at night.

Key Terms
تبيت النية (tabyit al-niyyah): The condition of making intention for fasting from the night before (i.e., before dawn).
المنذور المعين زمانه (al-nadhr al-mu'ayyan al-zaman): A vow that specifies a particular day or time for fasting.
نذر مطلق (nadhr mutlaq): An unconditional vow that does not specify a particular day; it becomes wajib upon fulfillment of a condition.
تعيين النية (ta'yin al-niyyah): Specification of the type of fast being intended (e.g., qada', kaffarah, etc.).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Qada' of Ramadan
B) Kaffarah fast
C) Nafl fast
D) Nadhr mutlaq fast

Key Takeaway
The intention conditions for fasting depend on the type: specified vows allow override by another wajib, while unspecified wajib fasts require both night intention and specification.

6 min
Establishing Ramadan and the Day of Doubt
Defining how the month of Ramadan and the day of doubt are established

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two methods of establishing Ramadan, define yawm al-shakk, and apply the Hanafi ruling that fasting the day of doubt as Ramadan is not allowed.

Core Idea
Ramadan begins by sighting or completing thirty days; the day of doubt is not to be fasted as Ramadan.

This passage from Thamaratun Najah explains how the month of Ramadan is confirmed and what the 'day of doubt' means. It provides two hadiths as evidence and clarifies the Hanafi position that fasting on the day of doubt as Ramadan is impermissible. Understanding these rules is essential for correctly observing the fast of Ramadan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
The passage gives two rulings: 1) Ramadan is established by sighting the crescent or completing thirty days of Sha'ban. 2) The day of doubt (30th Sha'ban) is defined, and by implication, fasting it as Ramadan is prohibited.
The act of establishing Ramadan is a condition for obligation; the act of fasting on yawm al-shakk is the subject of ikhtilaf.
2
Locate the dalil
Two hadiths are cited: from Ibn Umar (RA) and Abu Hurairah (RA), both commanding to fast upon sighting and to complete thirty days if obscured.
These hadiths are from Sahih al-Bukhari and Sahih Muslim, with qati' thubut (certain transmission) but zanni dalala (interpretation).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'fala tasumu hatta tarawhu' (do not fast until you see it) indicates prohibition of fasting before sighting. 'Akmalu al-idda thalathin' (complete the count thirty) shows that thirty days complete the month if sighting is impossible.
Thus, the proof directly links the ruling to sighting or completion.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some Shafi'i scholars permit fasting yawm al-shakk with the intention of Ramadan if it later turns out to be Ramadan, based on the sufficiency of intention.
The Hanafi school rejects this, citing the explicit prohibition of fasting before sighting.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school is that yawm al-shakk is not to be fasted as Ramadan; it is makruh tahrimi. Only habitual fasts or vows are permitted.
This tarjih is based on the strength of the hadith's prohibition.

Source Text

(بخاری شریف، باب قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا رأيتم الهلال فصوموا وإذا رأيتموه فأفطروا ص ٢٥٦، نمبر ١٩٠٧، مسلم شریف، باب وجوب صوم رمضان لرؤية الهلال ص ١٣٤٧، نمبر ٢٥١٦/١٠٨١) (۲) وعن أبي هريرة قال: قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: صوموا لرؤيته وأفطروا لرؤيته فإن أغمي عليكم فأكملوا عدة شعبان ثلاثين. (بخاری شریف، باب قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا رأيتم الهلال فصوموا ص ٢٥٦، نمبر ١٩٠٩، مسلم شریف، باب وجوب صوم رمضان لرؤية الهلال ص ١٣٤٧، نمبر ٢٥١٧/١٠٨١) ترجمه: (۱۰۱۱) اور ۲۹ شعبان سے جو دن ملا ہے وہ یوم شک ہے ( یعنی ۳۰ شعبان ) اور اس میں چاند کے ہونے نہ ہونے کا علم اور عدم علم برابر ہو اس طرح کہ چاند مشتبہ ہو۔ تشریح: اس عبارت میں یوم الشک کی توضیح ہے۔ ۳۰ شعبان کو یوم الشک کہتے ہیں، کیونکہ یہ معلوم نہیں ہے کہ ۲۹ شعبان کی
Section on what establishes the crescent and on fasting the day of doubt and others. (1010) Ramadan is established by sighting its crescent or after completing thirty of Sha'ban if the crescent is obscured. (1011) The day of doubt is that one day remains of Sha'ban and there is a hindrance in the sky preventing sighting, like clouds or dust, and it is ambiguous.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage deals with the beginning of Ramadan. (1010) states two ways Ramadan is confirmed: either by sighting the crescent on the night of 30 days (i.e., after 29 Sha'ban) or by completing thirty days of Sha'ban if the moon is obscured (ghamm). Two hadiths are cited to support this: from Ibn Umar (RA) that the month is twenty-nine nights, so do not fast until you see it, and if it is obscured, complete the count of thirty; and from Abu Hurairah (RA) with similar wording. The passage then defines the 'day of doubt' (yawm al-shakk) as the 30th of Sha'ban when sighting is hindered by clouds or dust, making the start of Ramadan ambiguous. The Hanafi position is that fasting on yawm al-shakk is not allowed unless it coincides with a habitual fast or one has a specific intention.

Key Terms
هلال (hilal): The new crescent moon of Ramadan that is sighted to mark the beginning of the month.
يوم الشك (yawm al-shakk): The 30th day of Sha'ban when the crescent is not seen due to obstruction, causing doubt about the start of Ramadan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How is Ramadan established if the crescent moon is not sighted on the 29th of Sha'ban?
A) By completing 30 days of Sha'ban
B) By calculation of astronomers
C) By the imam's announcement regardless of sighting
D) It cannot be established

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires actual sighting or completion of thirty days to confirm Ramadan; fasting on the day of doubt as Ramadan is prohibited.

Establishing the Start of Ramadan: Sighting or Completion of Thirty Days
Ruling on establishing Ramadan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two methods by which Ramadan is established according to the Hanafi school and explain the legal basis from the Sunnah.

Core Idea
Ramadan is confirmed by sighting the crescent or by completing thirty days of Sha'ban — no other method suffices.

This lesson introduces the foundational principle for determining the start of Ramadan. The Hanafi school, relying on a sound hadith, teaches that the month is established either by actual sighting of the new moon or by completing the month of Sha'ban as thirty days. This rule removes uncertainty and provides a clear criterion for the obligation of fasting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods
Look at the text: 'Ramadan is established by sighting its crescent or after completing thirty of Sha'ban if the crescent is hidden.' These are the only two ways to confirm the start of Ramadan.
Completing thirty days is a default when sighting is impossible.
2
Understand the proof from the Prophet's hadith
The text is based on the hadith: 'Fast when you see it (the crescent) and break your fast when you see it. If it is obscured, complete the count of Sha'ban to thirty.' This hadith establishes both sighting and completion as authoritative.
The imperative indicates obligation.
3
Apply the rule
If the moon is sighted on the 29th of Sha'ban, Ramadan begins that night. If not, Sha'ban is completed to thirty days, and Ramadan begins the next day. This removes doubt.
Also note that the month can never exceed thirty days.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : فيما يثبت به الهلال وفى صوم يوم الشك وغيره (۱۰۱۰) يثبت رمضان برؤية هلاله او بعد شعبان ثلاثين ان غُمَّ الهلال (۱۰۱۱) ويوم الشك هو مايلى التاسع والعشرين من شعبان وقد استولى فيه طرف العلم والجهل بان غُمَّ الهلال (۱۰۱۲) وكره فيه كل صوم الا صوم نفل جزم به بلا ترديد بينه وبين صوم آخر . وان ظهر انه من رمضان أجزأ عنه ما صامه (۱۰۱۳) وان ردّد فيه بين صيام و فطر لا يكون صائما (۱۰۱٤) وكره صوم يوم او يومين من اخر شعبان وما فوق ذلك فلا كراهة .
Chapter: On what establishes the new moon and on fasting on the day of doubt and other matters. (1010) Ramadan is established by sighting its crescent or after completing thirty days of Sha'ban if the crescent is hidden. (1011) The day of doubt is what follows the twenty-ninth of Sha'ban, and in it the two aspects of knowledge and ignorance are equal because the crescent was hidden. (1012) And it is disliked to fast on it except a nafl fast that is definitively intended without hesitation between it and another fast. And if it becomes apparent that it is from Ramadan, it suffices for what he fasted. (1013) And if he hesitates between fasting and breaking the fast, he is not considered a faster. (1014) And it is disliked to fast one or two days from the end of Sha'ban, and there is no dislike beyond that.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on establishing the new moon and rulings on the day of doubt. The author begins by stating that Ramadan is confirmed in two ways: by actual sighting of the crescent moon on the 29th of Sha'ban, or by completing 30 days of Sha'ban if the moon was not sighted. This is based on the hadith: 'Fast when you see it (the crescent) and break your fast when you see it, and if it is obscured, complete the count of Sha'ban to thirty.' The day of doubt is defined as the 30th of Sha'ban when the moon was not sighted and it is uncertain whether it is the first of Ramadan or not. The ruling is that fasting on this day is makruh (disliked) except if one makes a definite intention for a nafl (voluntary) fast without any hesitation that it might be Ramadan. If it later becomes clear that the day was indeed Ramadan, that nafl fast counts for Ramadan due to the principle of overlapping intentions. If one is undecided whether to fast or not (e.g., intending conditionally), then no fast is valid. Finally, it is makruh to fast one or two days at the end of Sha'ban as a precaution, but if one fasts more than two days (e.g., regularly fasting on Mondays and Thursdays), there is no dislike. The underlying reason is to avoid resembling the People of the Book who added to their fasts.

Key Terms
رؤية الهلال (ru'yah al-hilal): Sighting of the crescent moon, the primary method to establish the start of Ramadan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How is Ramadan established if the moon is not sighted on the 29th of Sha'ban?
A) By continuing to fast
B) By completing thirty days of Sha'ban
C) By relying on astronomical calculation
D) By waiting until the moon appears

Key Takeaway
Ramadan is established only by sighting the crescent or by completing thirty days of Sha'ban; no other method is valid in the Hanafi school.

Fasting on the Day of Doubt: Rulings and Intentions
Rulings on fasting on the day of doubt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the day of doubt, state the ruling on fasting on that day, and explain when a fast is valid or invalid.

Core Idea
Fasting on the day of doubt is disliked unless you make a definite intention for a nafl fast — if it turns out to be Ramadan, it counts.

Having learned how Ramadan is established, we now turn to the tricky situation when the 30th of Sha'ban arrives without sighting. This is the 'day of doubt'. The Hanafi school provides clear guidance: it is disliked to fast with an intention of Ramadan, but if one makes a firm intention for a voluntary fast, the fast is valid and, if it later becomes clear that it was Ramadan, it suffices. This lesson unpacks the rulings and their wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Define the day of doubt
The text defines it as the day following the 29th of Sha'ban when the crescent was hidden, so knowledge and ignorance are balanced.
It is the 30th day of Sha'ban, not a day earlier.
2
State the ruling on fasting: general rule
The text says: 'It is disliked to fast on it except a nafl fast that is definitively intended without hesitation.' So any fasting with an intention for Ramadan is makruh.
The dislike is due to uncertainty.
3
Understand the exception: definite nafl intention
If one makes a definite intention for a voluntary (nafl) fast without any hesitation between nafl and Ramadan, then the fast is not disliked. And if it turns out to be Ramadan, it suffices for the Ramadan fast.
This is based on the principle that overlapping intentions can be valid.
4
Identify invalid fasting: hesitation
If one is undecided whether to fast or not, or intends conditionally, then no fast is established.
Intention must be firm and unambiguous.
5
Note the ruling on fasting the last days of Sha'ban
It is disliked to fast one or two days at the end of Sha'ban (i.e., the 28th and 29th) as a precaution, but there is no dislike if one fasts more than two days, especially if it is a regular habit.
This prevents resembling the People of the Book.

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on establishing the new moon and rulings on the day of doubt. The author begins by stating that Ramadan is confirmed in two ways: by actual sighting of the crescent moon on the 29th of Sha'ban, or by completing 30 days of Sha'ban if the moon was not sighted. This is based on the hadith: 'Fast when you see it (the crescent) and break your fast when you see it, and if it is obscured, complete the count of Sha'ban to thirty.' The day of doubt is defined as the 30th of Sha'ban when the moon was not sighted and it is uncertain whether it is the first of Ramadan or not. The ruling is that fasting on this day is makruh (disliked) except if one makes a definite intention for a nafl (voluntary) fast without any hesitation that it might be Ramadan. If it later becomes clear that the day was indeed Ramadan, that nafl fast counts for Ramadan due to the principle of overlapping intentions. If one is undecided whether to fast or not (e.g., intending conditionally), then no fast is valid. Finally, it is makruh to fast one or two days at the end of Sha'ban as a precaution, but if one fasts more than two days (e.g., regularly fasting on Mondays and Thursdays), there is no dislike. The underlying reason is to avoid resembling the People of the Book who added to their fasts.

Key Terms
يوم الشك (yawm al-shakk): The 30th of Sha'ban when the new moon of Ramadan was not sighted, causing uncertainty.
صوم نفل (sawm nafal): Voluntary fast, not obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
On the day of doubt, certainty in intention is key: a definite nafl fast is valid and can count for Ramadan, but any hesitation or Ramadan-specific intention is makruh or invalid.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
8 min
Rulings on Fasting on the Day of Doubt (Yawm al-Shakk)
Explanation of prohibitions and conditions for fasting on yawm al-shakk with hadith proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fasting on yawm al-shakk, distinguish between valid and invalid intentions, and apply the exceptions for habitual nafl fasting.

Core Idea
Intent determines validity on the day of doubt — a firm nafl intention is valid; a conditional or hesitant intention invalidates the fast.

Fasting on the day when the new moon of Ramadan is uncertain (yawm al-shakk) requires precise intention. The Hanafi school, relying on clear hadith, distinguishes between a definitive nafl intention and a hesitant intention that nullifies the fast. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its nuances.

Guided Path
1
State the general ruling
Every fast on yawm al-shakk is disliked (makruh) except a definitive nafl fast that is firmly intended without any hesitation between it and another fast.
The exception is key — the intention must be unequivocal.
2
Identify the two types of intention
First type: unconditional intention for a nafl fast ('I will definitely fast a nafl fast'). Second type: conditional or hesitant intention ('If it is Ramadan, I am fasting; if not, I am not') or uncertainty between fasting and not fasting.
The second type does not constitute a valid intention.
3
Apply the ruling to the intention types
The first type (firm nafl) is not disliked (or lightly disliked) and is valid; if the day turns out to be Ramadan, it suffices. The second type results in no valid fast at all, whether it is Ramadan or not.
4
Note the exception for habitual fasting
If a person habitually fasts at the end of each month (e.g., fasting the 29th and 30th of Sha'ban), fasting on yawm al-shakk with a nafl intention is not disliked, based on the hadith exception.
This exception is directly from the hadith 'except a man who used to fast a fast'.
5
Locate the hadith proofs
The first hadith (Bukhari and Muslim) prohibits preceding Ramadan by a day or two of fasting, with the exception for habitual fasters. The second hadith (Abu Dawud and Tirmidhi) explicitly states that fasting on yawm al-shakk is disobedience to the Prophet.
These hadith provide the basis for the prohibition (karahah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيما يثبت به الهلال
مايـلـى التاسع والعشرين من شعبان وقد استولى فيه طرف العلم والجهل بان غُمَّ الهلال (١٠١٢) وكره فيه كل صوم الا صوم نفل جزم به بلا تردید بینه و بین صوم آخر . وان ظهر انه من رمضان أجزأ عنه ما صامه (١٠١٣) وان ردّد فيه بين صيام و فطر لا يكون صائما (١٠١٤) وكره صوم يوم شام کو کسی کو چاند نظر آیا ہے یا نہیں ، اور یہ شک کی کیفیت دو پہر تک رہتی ہے، دو پہر سے پہلے پہلے تک گواہی ہوگئی کہ کل چاند نظر آیا تھا تو آج پہلی رمضان ہے، اور گواہی نہیں ہوئی تو آج ٣٠ شعبان ہے، اب اس شک کی حالت میں روزہ رکھے یا نہیں، اور رکھے تو رمضان کی نیت کرے یا نفلی روزے کی ! آگے اس کی تفصیل ہے۔
ترجمه : (١٠١٢) اور شک کے دن میں ہر طرح کا روزہ مکروہ ہے مگر وہ یقینی نفل روزہ جس میں نفل اور دوسرا روزہ ( مثلا رمضان کے ) کے درمیان شک نہ ہو ۔ پس اگر یہ ظاہر ہو جائے کہ رمضان ہے تو جو روزہ رکھا ہے وہ رمضان کے لئے کافی ہے۔ تشریح : یہ عبارت پیچیدہ ہے۔ دو طرح کی نیت ہے [1] ٣٠ شعبان کی رات کو یہ نیت کی کہ میں حتمی طور پر نفلی روزہ رکھوں گا تو یہ روزہ مکروہ نہیں ہے۔ پس اگر ٣٠ شعبان ظاہر ہوا تو نفلی روزہ ہوگا ، اور اگر گواہی سے یکم رمضان طے ہو گیا تو پہلے گزر چکا ہے کہ نفل کی نیت سے بھی رمضان میں رمضان کا روزہ ہی ہو گا اس لئے رمضان کا روزہ ادا ہو جائے گا۔ [٢] اور اگر اس طرح کی اگر رمضان ہوگا تو رمضان کا روزہ رکھوں گا ، اور رمضان نہ ہوا تو نفلی روزہ رکھوں گا ، مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ صورت مکروہ ہے۔
ترجمه : (١٠١٣) ( اور اگر شک کے دن میں ) روزہ اور افطار کے درمیان میں شک ہے تو وہ روزہ رکھنے والا شمار نہیں ہوگا۔ تشریح : اس طرح نیت کی کہ اگر رمضان ہوا تو روزے سے رہوں گا، اور رمضان نہیں ہوا تو روزہ ہی نہیں رکھوں گا ، اس صورت میں روزہ رکھنے اور نہ رکھنے میں تردد ہے اس لئے روزے کی نیت ہی نہیں ہوئی اس لئے رمضان ہوا تب بھی رمضان کا روزہ نہیں ہوگا، اور رمضان نہیں ہوا تو نفل کا روزہ بھی نہیں ہوگا، کیونکہ نیت ہی نہیں ہے۔ ترجمه : (١٠١٤) اور شعبان کے اخیر میں ایک یا دو دن کے روزے مکروہ ہیں ، اور (اخیر کے ایک دو دن سے ) اوپر کے روزے میں کراہت نہیں۔
تشریح : شعبان کے آخیر میں ایک دوروزے رکھنے کے دو طریقے ہیں [1] رمضان کی نیت سے رکھے تو بالکل جائز نہیں ہے کیونکہ یہود نے فرض روزے میں اضافہ کیا اور بعد میں تمام روزے چھوڑ دئے ، اسی طرح مسلمان بھی رمضان سے پہلے اضافہ کرلے اس لئے جائز نہیں ہے۔[٢] دوسرا طریقہ یہ ہے کہ نفل کی نیت سے روزہ رکھے ، اس میں بھی اضافہ کی مشابہت ہے اس لئے یہ بھی مکروہ ہے ۔ ہاں جس آدمی کی عادت ہو کہ ہر مہینے کے آخیر میں ایک دو روزے رکھتے ہوں اس کے لئے گنجائش ہے کہ وہ یہ روزے رکھ لے۔ دو دن سے زیادہ نفلی روزہ ہر آدمی رکھ سکتا ہے، کیونکہ اس میں اضافہ کا شبہ نہیں ہے۔
حدیث میں ہے۔ عن ابي هريرة عن النبى الله أنه قال : لا يتقدمن أحدكم رمضان بصوم يوم أو يومين الا أن يكون رجل كان يصوم صوما فليصم ذالک اليوم . (بخاری شریف، باب لا يتقدم رمضان بصوم یوم و لا یومین، ص ١٣٠٧ نمبر ١٩١٤ر مسلم شریف ، باب لا نقدر موار مضان بصوم یوم ولا یومین ، ص ٤٤١ نمبر ٢٥١٨/١٠٨٢) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ رمضان سے ایک دن پہلے جو شک کا دن ہوتا ہے اس دن رمضان کا روزہ نہ رکھے، البتہ کسی کو مہینے کے آخیر میں نفلی روزہ رکھنے کی عادت ہو تو اس کو اجازت ہے۔ (٢) یوم الشک میں عام لوگوں کے لئے روزہ رکھنا مکروہ ہے اس کے لئے یہ حدیث ہے . عن صلة قال كنا عند عمار فى اليوم الذى يشك فيه فأتى بشاة فتنحى بعض القوم فقال عمار : من صام هذا اليوم فقد عصى أبا القاسم صلى الله عليه وسلم (ابوداود شریف، باب کراحیة صوم یوم الشک ، ص ١٣٤٠، نمبر ٢٣٣٤ / ترندی شریف ، باب ما جاء فی کراھیة صوم یوم الشک ، ص ١٧٥، نمبر ٦٦٨) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ شک کے دن روزہ رکھے تو حضور کی نافرمانی ہوگی۔
Chapter on what establishes the crescent moon. What follows the twenty-ninth of Sha'ban when both knowledge and ignorance prevail due to the moon being obscured (1012) And every fast is disliked therein except a nafl fast that is firmly intended without doubt between it and another fast. And if it becomes apparent that it is from Ramadan, what he fasted suffices for it. (1013) And if he is uncertain between fasting and breaking fast, he is not considered a faster. (1014) And fasting on the day of doubt is disliked – meaning whether anyone saw the moon or not, and this state of doubt lasts until mid-afternoon. If before mid-afternoon testimony comes that the moon was seen yesterday, then today is the first of Ramadan; if no testimony comes, then today is the 30th of Sha'ban. Now in this state of doubt, should one fast or not, and if he fasts, should he intend Ramadan or nafl? Its detail follows. Translation: (1012) And on the day of doubt, every kind of fast is disliked except a definitive nafl fast in which there is no doubt between nafl and another fast (e.g. Ramadan). So if it becomes apparent that it is Ramadan, the fast he kept suffices for Ramadan. Explanation: This statement is complex. There are two types of intention: [1] On the night of the 30th of Sha'ban, he intended: 'I will definitely fast a nafl fast' – this fast is not disliked. Then if it becomes clear it is the 30th of Sha'ban, it will be a nafl fast; and if by testimony the 1st of Ramadan is determined, it already passed that even with the intention of nafl, in Ramadan it will be a Ramadan fast anyway, so the Ramadan fast will be fulfilled. [2] And if he intends such that: 'If it is Ramadan, I will fast a Ramadan fast; and if it is not Ramadan, I will fast a nafl fast' – the author says this case is disliked. Translation: (1013) (And on the day of doubt) if he is uncertain between fasting and breaking fast, he will not be counted as a faster. Explanation: He intended such that: 'If it is Ramadan, I will remain fasting; and if it is not Ramadan, I will not fast at all' – in this case there is hesitation between fasting and not fasting, so no intention of fasting was made; therefore even if it is Ramadan, it will not be a Ramadan fast; and if it is not Ramadan, it will not be a nafl fast either, because there is no intention. Translation: (1014) And fasting one or two days at the end of Sha'ban is disliked, and above (more than one or two) there is no dislike. Explanation: There are two methods of fasting one or two days at the end of Sha'ban: [1] If he intends Ramadan, it is absolutely not permissible because the Jews made an addition to the obligatory fast and later abandoned all fasts; similarly, if a Muslim adds before Ramadan, it is not permissible. [2] The second method is to intend nafl fast – this also resembles addition, so it is also disliked. However, for a person who habitually fasts one or two days at the end of every month, there is leeway for him to fast these days. Fasting more than two days as nafl is permissible for anyone, because there is no suspicion of addition. There is a hadith: From Abu Hurairah from the Prophet ﷺ that he said: 'Do not precede Ramadan by fasting a day or two, except a man who used to fast a fast, so let him fast that day.' (Bukhari, Bab la yataqaddam Ramadan bi sawm yawm wa la yawmayn, p. 1307 no. 1914; Muslim, Bab la yataqaddam ramadan bi sawm yawm wa la yawmayn, p. 441 no. 2518/1082) This hadith means: on the day before Ramadan which is the day of doubt, do not fast intending Ramadan; but if someone has a habit of fasting nafl at the end of the month, he is permitted. (2) Fasting on the day of doubt is disliked for common people; for this there is this hadith: From Silah who said: We were with Ammar on the day that is doubted, and a sheep was brought; some people moved away, and Ammar said: 'Whoever fasts this day has disobeyed Abu al-Qasim ﷺ.' (Abu Dawud, Bab Karahiyat Sawm Yawm al-Shakk, p. 1340 no. 2334; Tirmidhi, Bab ma ja'a fi Karahiyat Sawm Yawm al-Shakk, p. 175 no. 668) This hadith means: if one fasts on the day of doubt, he disobeys the Prophet ﷺ.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof on fasting during the day of doubt (yawm al-shakk) – the 30th of Sha'ban when it is uncertain whether the new moon has been sighted. The author first states the general ruling: every fast on this day is makruh (disliked) except a definite nafl fast intended without any hesitation between it and another fast. If it later becomes clear that the day was actually Ramadan, that nafl fast will count for Ramadan because the intention of nafl is subsumed under the greater obligation. However, if the intention is conditional – 'if it is Ramadan then I am fasting, otherwise not' – then no valid fast occurs, even if it was Ramadan, because the intention is indeterminate. The same applies if one is simply uncertain between fasting and not fasting. Then the author addresses fasting one or two days at the end of Sha'ban. Such fasting is makruh whether intended as Ramadan or nafl, because it resembles the practice of the Jews who added to the obligatory fast. However, if a person has a habitual pattern of fasting at the end of each month, an exception is made. Fasting more than two days is not disliked because the resemblance to addition disappears. The author supports the ruling with two hadith: the first (from Bukhari and Muslim) forbids preceding Ramadan by fasting a day or two, except for one who regularly fasts. The second (from Abu Dawud and Tirmidhi) explicitly states that fasting on yawm al-shakk is disobedience to the Prophet. Thus, the core of this passage is to establish the prohibition (karahah) of fasting on the day of doubt with any intention of Ramadan or conditional intention, while allowing a firm nafl intention for those who habitually fast. The discussion is purely Hanafi, with no reference to other madhhabs.

Key Terms
یوم الشک (yawm al-shakk): The day of doubt – the 30th of Sha'ban when the sighting of the new moon of Ramadan is uncertain until mid-afternoon.
صوم نفل (sawm al-nafl): A supererogatory fast – voluntary fasting not obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on fasting on the day of doubt (yawm al-shakk) with the intention: 'If this is Ramadan, I am fasting; if not, I am not fasting'?
A) The fast is valid as Ramadan if it is Ramadan, and as nafl if it is not.
B) The fast is makruh but valid if it turns out to be Ramadan.
C) No fast is considered to have occurred, even if it is Ramadan.
D) The fast is obligatory to make up later.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a fast on the day of doubt depends entirely on the intention: a firm unconditional nafl intention is valid, while any hesitation or condition renders the fast null.

Rulings for the Mufti, Qadi, and the Individual Seeing the Moon on Yawm al-Shakk
Instructions for mufti, qadi, khawass, and lone moon-sighter on yawm al-shakk

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for the mufti, qadi, khawass, and lone moon-sighter on yawm al-shakk and distinguish the applicable categories.

Core Idea
The ruling on yawm al-shakk differs based on knowledge: commoners break, elites fast, and the lone sighter is personally bound.

On the day of doubt (yawm al-shakk) – the 30th of Sha'ban when Ramadan's arrival is uncertain – the Hanafi school prescribes different actions based on the individual's capacity to maintain a valid intention. This passage distinguishes three categories: the general public, the learned elite (including the mufti and qadi), and the person who personally sights the crescent but whose testimony is rejected. Each has a distinct ruling, reflecting the school's concern for both communal order and individual certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of people
Note the common public (عامة), the elite (خواص – including mufti and qadi), and the lone moon-sighter (من رأى الهلال وحده). Each has a different ruling.
The khawass are defined as those who can control their intention without hesitation.
2
Extract the ruling for the common public
The mufti commands them to wait (talammuth) during the morning. If no testimony arrives by the time for intention expires, they must break fast (i.e., not fast).
Talammuth is a recommended waiting period, not a full day's fast.
3
Extract the ruling for the elite
The mufti, qadi, and those of similar knowledge may fast on yawm al-shakk because they can maintain a correct intention (either as nafl or as Ramadan if it turns out to be Ramadan).
They are exempt from the prohibition because their knowledge prevents hesitation.
4
Extract the ruling for the lone moon-sighter
If someone alone sees the crescent of Ramadan or Eid and his testimony is rejected by the court, he is personally obliged to fast (if Ramadan) or break fast (if Eid). His personal certainty binds him.
The phrase 'in one aspect' (وجه) indicates a nuance: the obligation is personal, not communal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (۱۰۱۵) ويؤمر المفتى العامة بالتلوم يوم الشك ثم بالافطار اذا ذهب وقت النية ولم يتعيّن الحال (۱۰۱٦) ويصوم فيه المفتى والقاضي. ومن كان من الخواص وهو ما يتمكن من ضبط نفسه عن التردد في النية وملاحظة كونه عن الفرض . (١٠١۷) ومن رأى هلال رمضان او الفطر وحده وردّ قوله لزمه الصيام وجه:
Translation: (1015) And the mufti commands the common people to wait on the day of doubt, then to break fast when the time for intention has passed and the situation has not become clear. (1016) And the mufti and the qadi fast on it. Also those who are of the elite (khawass) – that is, one who can control himself from hesitation in intention and observance of whether it is obligatory. (1017) And whoever sees the crescent of Ramadan or Eid alone and his word is rejected, fasting becomes obligatory for him in one aspect.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage provides specific rulings for different categories of people on yawm al-shakk. First, the mufti (jurisconsult) should instruct the general public to wait (talammuth) during the morning of the 30th of Sha'ban, hoping that a witness may come. If no testimony arrives by the time the intention for fasting expires (i.e., mid-afternoon or earlier, depending on the school's view on niyyah time), the mufti should command them to break fast (i.e., not to fast that day). This is because the common people are not well-versed in the fine points of intention and may fall into error. Second, the mufti himself, the qadi (judge), and the khawass (elite scholars) are permitted to fast on yawm al-shakk. The khawass are defined as those who can control their intention so that they do not waver between fasting as nafl or as Ramadan, and who are aware of when the fast becomes obligatory (i.e., if it turns out to be Ramadan, they know it counts). This superior knowledge allows them to fast without falling into the prohibited hesitation. Third, a person who alone sights the crescent of Ramadan or Eid, but whose testimony is rejected by the court (e.g., due to insufficient witnesses or lack of legal competence), is still obliged to fast (if it is Ramadan) or not to fast (if it is Eid) individually, because his own certainty binds him. The phrase 'in one aspect' (وجه) indicates a legal nuance that may be elaborated elsewhere. These rulings are all part of the Hanafi position on yawm al-shakk, emphasizing the distinction between the common public and the learned elite.

Key Terms
التلوم (al-talammuth): Waiting and lingering in expectation of testimony during the morning of yawm al-shakk.
الخواص (al-khawass): The elite – those with detailed knowledge of fiqh who can avoid hesitation in intention and know the exact conditions of obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What should the mufti instruct the general public to do on the day of doubt if no witness comes by the time the intention for fasting expires?
A) Continue fasting anyway.
B) Break the fast (i.e., not fast).
C) Delay the intention until after mid-afternoon.
D) Repeat the testimony process.

Key Takeaway
On yawm al-shakk, the Hanafi school differentiates: commoners break fast after the waiting period, the elite may fast, and the lone moon-sighter must act on his certainty.

The Day of Doubt: Ruling for the General Public
States the ruling for the common people on the day of doubt with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fasting on the day of doubt (yawm al-shakk) for the general public and explain the procedure of talawwum and the proof from hadith.

Core Idea
On the day of doubt, the general public must wait until midday; if no crescent is confirmed, they break fast, as fasting that day is makruh and disobedient.

Fasting on the day of doubt (the 30th of Sha'ban without confirmed sighting) is a case where precaution meets the Sunnah. The Hanafi school requires the mufti to instruct the public to wait (talawwum) until midday, and if no witness arrives, they must break the fast. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its evidence, and the rationale behind the dislike of fasting on that day.

Guided Path
1
Define the day of doubt
Identify yawm al-shakk as the 30th of Sha'ban when the crescent of Ramadan is not sighted. Understand why it is a day of uncertainty.
The Hanafi definition emphasizes the absence of certainty.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for the public
The mufti orders the general public to wait (al-talawwum) until the time of intention for fasting ends (Zuhr). If a witness comes by then, they fast; otherwise, they break fast.
This ruling applies to the common people, not those with a habit of fasting.
3
Examine the hadith proofs
Analyze the two hadith: (1) 'Do not precede Ramadan by fasting a day or two…' (Bukhari, Muslim) and (2) 'Whoever fasts this day has disobeyed Abu al-Qasim' (Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi). Note the prohibitive language and its interpretation as dislike (karahah).
The second hadith explicitly mentions the day of doubt.
4
Distinguish exceptions
Recognize that the hadith makes an exception for someone who habitually fasts on that day (e.g., every Monday). For such a person, fasting is permissible as nafl.
The exception prevents the ruling from being absolute prohibition.

Source Text

فصل فيما يثبت به الهلال
او يومين من آخر شعبان لا يكره ما فوقها (۱۰۱۵) ويؤمر المفتى العامة بالتلوم يوم الشك ثم بالافطار اذا ذهب وقت النية ولم يتعيّن الحال
وجه : (۱) اس حدیث میں ہے۔ عن ابي هريرة عن النبى الله أنه قال : لا يتقدمن أحدكم رمضان بصوم يوم أو يومين الا أن يكون رجل كان يصوم صوما فليصم ذالک اليوم . (بخاری شریف، باب لا يتقدم رمضان بصوم یوم و لا یومین، ص ۱۳۰۷ نمبر ۱۹۱۴ر مسلم شریف ، باب لا نقدر موار مضان بصوم یوم ولا یومین ، ص ۴۴۱ نمبر ۲۵۱۸/۱۰۸۲) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ رمضان سے ایک دن پہلے جو شک کا دن ہوتا ہے اس دن رمضان کا روزہ نہ رکھے، البتہ کسی کو مہینے کے آخیر میں نفلی روزہ رکھنے کی عادت ہو تو اس کو اجازت ہے۔ (۲) یوم الشک میں عام لوگوں کے لئے روزہ رکھنا مکروہ ہے اس کے لئے یہ حدیث ہے . عن صلة قال كنا عند عمار فى اليوم الذى يشك فيه فأتى بشاة فتنحى بعض القوم فقال عمار : من صام هذا اليوم فقد عصى أبا القاسم الله الله (ابوداود شریف، باب کراحیة صوم یوم الشک ، ص ۱۳۴۰، نمبر ۲۳۳۴ / ترندی شریف ، باب ما جاء فی کراھیة صوم یوم الشک ، ص ۱۷۵، نمبر ۶ ۶۸ ) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ شک کے دن روزہ رکھے تو حضور کی نافرمانی ہوگی۔
ترجمه : (۱۰۱۵) اور مفتی یوم شک میں عام لوگوں کو انتظار کا حکم دے گا ، پھر جب نیت کا وقت ختم ہو جائے اور کوئی حالت متعین نہ ہو تو افطار ( کا حکم دیں) تشریح : ۳۰ شعبان کی صبح کو جو شک کا دن ہے اور شک کا وقت ہے مفتیان کرام عام لوگوں کو انتظار کا حکم دیں تا کہ دو پہر تک کوئی گواہ آ جائے تو رمضان کا روزہ رکھے اور دو پہر تک نہ آئے تو افطار کا حکم دیں۔
Section on what establishes the crescent. ... and it is commanded that the mufti orders the general public to wait on the day of doubt, then to break fast when the time of intention has gone and the situation is not determined. Proof (1): This hadith... (2): Fasting on the day of doubt is disliked for the general public... Translation: (1015) And the mufti orders the general public to wait on the day of doubt, then when the time of intention ends and the situation is not determined, he orders them to break fast. Explanation: ...
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the day of doubt (yawm al-shakk) for the general public. The author first states the ruling: the mufti should order the common people to wait (al-talawwum) on the day of doubt until midday. If a witness comes by then, they fast; otherwise, they break fast. The proof is from two hadith: one prohibiting fasting a day or two before Ramadan, and another explicitly stating that fasting on the day of doubt is disobedience to the Prophet (pbuh). The Urdu commentary clarifies that the waiting period is until Zuhr time. The ruling is makruh for the public to fast on that day as a precaution.

Key Terms
یوم الشک (yawm al-shakk): The day of doubt, i.e. the 30th of Sha'ban when the crescent of Ramadan is not sighted.
التلوم (al-talawwum): Waiting until midday (Zuhr time) for a witness to confirm the crescent sighting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) They may fast if they wish.
D) Fasting is forbidden for them.

Key Takeaway
The general public must not fast on the day of doubt; they wait until midday and break fast if no crescent is confirmed, as fasting on that day is makruh tanzih.

The Elite’s Ruling on the Day of Doubt
Defines the elite and states their permissibility to fast on yawm al-shakk

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'al-khawass' in the context of yawm al-shakk and state the Hanafi ruling for their fast.

Core Idea
The elite may fast on the day of doubt because they can maintain a pure nafl intention without ambiguity.

In the last lesson, we learned that the general public must not fast on the day of doubt. But the Hanafi school carves out an exception for the learned. This passage introduces the category of 'al-khawass' (the elite) and clarifies that muftis, qadis, and those with deep knowledge of fasting rules may fast as nafl. The key is their ability to guard the intention from any hesitation that would corrupt it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Special Subject
The act is fasting on yawm al-shakk (30th Sha'ban when Ramadan is doubtful). The passage specifies that the mufti, qadi, and the elite (al-khawass) are permitted to fast.
2
Define 'al-Khawass'
The text defines them as those who can control themselves from hesitation in intention (tardud fi al-niyyah) and from observing whether the fast becomes obligatory (i.e., if it later turns out to be Ramadan).
This implies they know the fiqh details of intention and can avoid mixing nafl with obligatory intention.
3
Identify the Condition for Permissibility
They must be able to maintain a true nafl intention without any ambiguity that they might be fasting for Ramadan. This requires knowledge of the rules.
If they cannot do this, they fall under the general prohibition.
4
State the Hukm and Its Rationale
The ruling is mubah (permissible) for them as nafl. The rationale is that they are immune to the problem that justifies the general karahah: they will not inadvertently intend Ramadan and thus mix the two.
This is a concession for the learned.

Source Text
